Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Ash'ari Position on Obligating the Impossible: A Hanafi Defence
Usul dispute: refuting the charge that Ash'ari theology necessitates obligating the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether the Ash'ari principles of power with the act and creation of acts necessarily entail obligating the impossible, and explain the Hanafi author's rebuttal using the concepts of timing of capacity and acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
Obligating the impossible is not necessary from Ash'ari principles when capacity is required at performance and obligation targets acquisition.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary on Musallam al-Thubut addresses a classic usul dispute: does the Ash'ari theological stance on human power and divine creation logically force the conclusion that Allah can obligate the impossible (taklif al-muhal)? Critics argued yes, but the author argues no. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyse how theological premises affect usul reasoning and to distinguish between apparent logical consequences and actual entailments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Ash'ari principles under attack
The passage begins by stating two principles: (1) power (qudra) is simultaneous with the act, (2) all acts of servants are created by Allah. Critics claim these lead to obligating the impossible. Note the exact wording in Arabic.
The Arabic phrases are 'كون القدرة مع الفعل' and 'كون أفعال العباد مخلوقة لله تعالى'.
2
Follow the critics' reasoning
For principle (1): since power does not exist before the act, at the time of obligation (before act) the act is impossible. For principle (2): if acts are created by Allah, they are not within the servant's power. Trace these arguments in the text.
The critics' conclusion: 'صار الفعل عند التكليف غير مقدور للعبد' and 'لم تكن مقدورة للمكلف'.
3
Examine the author's response for each principle
The author says the charge is not necessary. For (1): capacity is required at the time of performance (zaman al-iqa'), not obligation. For (2): obligation relates only to acquisition (kasb), not creation (khalq). Kasb is possible for the servant. Note the author's precise language: 'القدرة إنما تجب في زمان الإيقاع' and 'التكليف عنده لا يتعلق إلا بالكسب'.
The author repeatedly states 'الحق أنه ليس بلازم' (the truth is it is not necessary).
4
Analyse the second argument from the Abu Jahl case
The Ash'aris also cite the case of Abu Jahl: Allah commanded him to believe, yet revealed he would not. Critics say this is obligating the impossible. The author responds that the obligation was only on general belief in Sharia rulings, not on believing the specific report of his disbelief. The possible does not become impossible by divine knowledge.
Key phrase: 'لا تكليف لأبي جهل إلا بالتصديق في أحكام الشرع'.
5
Consider the author's critique of alternative answers
The author mentions and rejects two other responses: (a) that if Abu Jahl knew he would not believe, obligation drops (rejected: man is not left neglected); (b) that he is obligated to believe in summary (rejected because it would entail actual belief). Understand why these are insufficient.
The author says about (a): 'ممنوع' and about (b): 'محال منه'.

أما لزوم التكليف بالمحال من الأصل الأول (كون القدرة مع الفعل) فلأنه لما لم تكن القدرة قبل الفعل، وحال التكليف الذي هو قبل الفعل صار الفعل عند التكليف غير مقدور للعبد ومستحيلاً بالنسبة إليه. وأما لزوم التكليف بالمحال من الأصل الثاني كون أفعال العباد مخلوقةً لله تعالى فلأن أفعال العباد إذا كانت مخلوقة لله تعالى وتحت قدرته لم تكن مقدورة للمكلف، فاستحالت صدورها منه، بل التزمت الأشعرية التكليف بالمحال على أنفسهم يعني يقولون: لا بأس به، وأقوال الإمام الأشعري المنقولة عنه في كتب الكلام والأصول تدل على جوازه عنده. جواب المصنف عما ألزموا على الأشعري: والحق أنه ليس بلازم. أما من الأول فلأن القدرة إنما تجب في زمان الإيقاع، حتى يتحقق الامتثال لا زمان التكليف. وأما من الثاني فلأن التكليف عنده لا يتعلق إلا بالكسب، لا بالإيجاد. وفيه كلام في الكلام. قال: والحق أنه ليس بلازم (من الأصلين المذكورين) فالتزامهم من غير لزوم. فأما عدم لزوم التكليف بالمحال من الأصل الأول، فلأن القدرة إنما تجب في زمان إيقاع الفعل، وفي حين إيجاده، حتى يتحقق الامتثال بإتيان الفعل، ولا يجب وجود القدرة في زمان التكليف، فلم يلزم التكليف بما هو غير مقدور حين إيجاد الفعل. وأما عدم اللزوم من الأصل الثاني فلأن التكليف عنده (عند الأشعري) لا يتعلق إلا بالكسب (دون الخلق) وهو (الكسب) فعل مقدور للمكلف، ولا يتعلق التكليف بالإيجاد والخلق الذي هو غير مقدور له. وفي تعلق التكليف بالكسب دون الخلق كلام دقيق في علم الكلام طويل ذيله.
الدليل الثاني للأشاعرة على جواز التكليف بالممتنع: ثانياً: كلف الله أبا جهل بالإيمان، وهو التصديق بما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ومما جاء به أنه لا يصدقه، فقد كلفه بأن يصدقه في أن لا يصدقه، وهو إنما يكون بانتفاء التصديق، إذ لو كان لعلم. والجواب: أن لا تكليف إلا بالتصديق في أحكام الشرع، وعدم التصديق إخبار منه تعالى إليه، ولا يخرج الممكن عن الإمكان بعلم أو خبر. وقالوا المجوزون التكليف بالمحال ثانياً: وقد كلف الله تعالى أبا جهل بالإيمان، وهو التصديق بجميع ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ومما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن أبا جهل لا يصدق بما جاء به، فقد كلف الله أبا جهل بأن يصدق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في أنه لا يصدقه فيما جاء به، وهو (أي تصديقه) إنما يكون بانتفاء تصديقه، أي تصديقه في أن لا يصدقه يستلزم أن لا يصدقه؛ إذ لو كان منه التصديق لعلمه؛ لأن كل عاقل يعلم التصديق الواقع منه إذا توجه إليه فلا يعادي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. والجواب عنه: أنه لا تكليف لأبي جهل وأمثاله من العصاة إلا بالتصديق في أحكام الشرع، بأنها من عند الله وبأخباره بالبعث والنشور، والجنة والنار، وعذاب القبر، وعدم التصديق إخبار منه تعالى إلى النبي، وأبو جهل غير مكلف بتصديق هذا الإخبار، فلم يكلف بتصديق عدم تصديق فلا استحالة، ولا يخرج التصديق الممكن عن الإمكان بعلمه تعالى وإخباره.
نقل جوابان آخران والرد عليهما: وما قيل: لو علم أبو جهل يسقط عنه التكليف ممنوع، فإن الإنسان لم يترك سدى. قيل في الجواب: إنه مكلف بالتصديق بالجميع إجمالاً، والتصديق بعدم التصديق إنما يستلزم عدم التصديق إذا كان تفصيلاً. أقول: التصديق بالجميع إجمالاً محال منه؛ لأنه يتحقق التصديق منه، وفرض أنه لا تصديق منه، فتدبر. (وما قيل في الجواب عن الدليل الثاني للأشعري: لو علم أبو جهل أنه
As for the necessity of obligating the impossible from the first principle (that power is together with the act): because when power did not exist before the act, and the state of obligation which is before the act, the act at the time of obligation became beyond the slave's ability and impossible relative to him. As for the necessity of obligating the impossible from the second principle that the acts of servants are created by Allah: because if the acts of servants are created by Allah and under His power, they are not possible for the obligated person, so their occurrence from him becomes impossible. Rather, the Ash'aris committed themselves to obligating the impossible, meaning they say: there is nothing wrong with it, and the statements of Imam al-Ash'ari transmitted from him in books of theology and usul indicate its permissibility according to him. The author's response to what they imposed on al-Ash'ari: the truth is that it is not necessary. As for the first: because power is only required at the time of performance, so that compliance is achieved, not at the time of obligation. As for the second: because obligation according to him only relates to acquisition (kasb), not to creation. And there is discussion in theology. He said: The truth is that it is not necessary (from the two mentioned principles), so their commitment is without necessity. As for the non-necessity of obligating the impossible from the first principle: because power is only required at the time of performing the act, and at the moment of its bringing into existence, so that compliance is achieved by doing the act, and the existence of power at the time of obligation is not required, so there is no necessary obligation of what is not possible at the time of performing the act. As for the non-necessity from the second principle: because obligation according to him (al-Ash'ari) only relates to acquisition (without creation), and it (acquisition) is an act possible for the obligated person, and obligation does not relate to bringing into existence and creation which is not possible for him. And regarding the relation of obligation to acquisition without creation, there is precise discussion in theology with a long tail. The second proof of the Ash'aris on the permissibility of obligating the impossible: Secondly: Allah obligated Abu Jahl to believe, which is affirmation of what the Prophet brought, and among what he brought is that he does not affirm him, so He obligated him to affirm him regarding his not affirming him, which is only by the negation of affirmation, for if it were, he would know. The answer: There is no obligation except affirmation in the rulings of the Sharia, and the negation of affirmation is a report from Allah to him, and the possible does not leave possibility by knowledge or report. And the permitters of obligating the impossible said secondly: And Allah has obligated Abu Jahl to believe, which is affirmation of all that the Prophet brought, and among what the Prophet brought is that Abu Jahl does not affirm what he brought, so Allah obligated Abu Jahl to affirm the Prophet regarding his not affirming him in what he brought, and it (i.e., his affirmation) is only by the negation of his affirmation, i.e., his affirmation regarding his not affirming him necessitates that he does not affirm him; for if he had affirmation, he would know it, because every rational person knows the affirmation occurring from himself when he attends to it, so he would not oppose the Prophet. The answer about it: There is no obligation upon Abu Jahl and similar sinners except affirmation in the rulings of the Sharia, that they are from Allah and His reports of resurrection, Paradise, Hell, grave punishment, and the negation of affirmation is a report from Allah to the Prophet, and Abu Jahl is not obligated to affirm this report, so he was not obligated to affirm the negation of affirmation, so there is no impossibility, and the possible affirmation does not leave possibility by His knowledge and report. Two other answers are transmitted and reply to them: And what was said: If Abu Jahl knew, the obligation would drop from him, is denied, for man is not left neglected. It was said in answer: He is obligated to affirm all in summary, and affirmation of the negation of affirmation only necessitates the negation of affirmation if it is in detail. I say: Affirmation of all in summary is impossible from him, because his affirmation would be realized, and it is assumed that there is no affirmation from him, so ponder. (And what was said in answer to the second proof of al-Ash'ari: If Abu Jahl knew that...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi commentary on a usul dispute concerning the Ash'ari position on obligating the impossible (تكليف المحال). The author first presents two principles from which critics argued that the Ash'ari view necessarily leads to obligating the impossible: (1) that power (قدرة) is simultaneous with the act, so at the time of obligation (before the act) the act is not within the servant's ability; (2) that all acts of servants are created by Allah, so they are not possible for the servant. The author clarifies that these do not actually obligate تكليف المحال. For the first, capacity is only needed at the time of performing the act (زمان الإيقاع), not at the time of obligation. For the second, obligation according to al-Ash'ari pertains only to acquisition (كسب), not to creation (خلق); the servant's acquisition is possible for him. Then the author addresses a second argument from the Ash'aris: that Allah obligated Abu Jahl to believe, yet from the same revelation it is known that Abu Jahl would not believe, thus obligating the impossible. The response is that the obligation was only on general belief in the Sharia rulings (تصديق في أحكام الشرع), not on believing the specific report about his own disbelief. The author also mentions two other attempted answers and critiques them: one saying that if Abu Jahl knew he wouldn't believe, obligation would drop—this is rejected because man is not left neglected; another saying he is obligated to believe in summary (إجمالاً), but the author finds this impossible because it would entail actual belief. The text demonstrates a nuanced defense of Ash'ari theology by showing that their positions do not inevitably lead to تكليف المحال when correctly interpreted.

Key Terms
تكليف المحال (taklīf al-muḥāl): Obligating an impossible act, i.e., commanding something that cannot be done by the obligated person.
الكسب (al-kasb): The servant's acquisition of an act, which is distinct from the divine creation of the act; it is the aspect under which the act is attributed to the servant and thus possible for him.
القدرة (al-qudra): The capacity or power to perform an act; according to the author, it is required at the time of performing the act, not at the time of obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the Ash'ari principle that power is simultaneous with the act not lead to obligating the impossible?
A) Because power is required at the time of obligation, not at the time of performance.
B) Because the obligated person can acquire the act through kasb.
C) Because power is only required at the time of performing the act, not at the time of obligation.
D) Because Allah can obligate whatever He wills without conditions.

Key Takeaway
The charge that Ash'ari theology necessitates obligating the impossible is not logically required when capacity is tied to performance and obligation to acquisition (kasb).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
15 min
Refutation of Taklif bil-Muhal: The Case of Abu Jahl
Refuting the second argument of those who permit impossible obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the Ash'ari argument of taklif bil-muhal using the case of Abu Jahl, and identify the key distinction between types of tasdiq.

Core Idea
Allah does not obligate the impossible: the obligation on Abu Jahl was only for ahkam, not for the khabr of his disbelief.

This passage addresses a critical dispute in usul al-fiqh: can Allah command something impossible? The Ash'ari school argues yes, citing the case of Abu Jahl. The Hanafi author refutes this by restricting the object of obligation, showing that no contradiction exists. Understanding this refutation clarifies the Hanafi principle that obligation is contingent on capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's argument
Read the passage to extract the Ash'ari argument: Allah obligated Abu Jahl to believe (tasdiq) in everything the Prophet brought, including the report that he would not believe. This creates a contradiction.
Focus on the logical structure: obligation to believe entails believing that one will not believe.
2
Locate the Hanafi response
Find the author's answer: The obligation is only on tasdiq in the rulings of the law (ahkam al-shar'), not on tasdiq of every report (khabr). The report of Abu Jahl's disbelief is not within the scope of obligation.
The key phrase: 'لا تكليف لأبي جهل ... إلا بالتصديق في أحكام الشرع'
3
Analyze the distinction
Understand that the Hanafi distinction rests on separating tasdiq in ahkam (e.g., belief in resurrection, paradise) from tasdiq in akhbar (e.g., reports about individuals). The latter is not obligatory.
This avoids the impossible requirement to simultaneously affirm and deny.
4
Evaluate the supplementary answers
The author presents and refutes two alternative answers: (1) knowledge removes obligation, (2) general (ijmal) affirmation avoids contradiction. Note why each is rejected.
First is rejected because man is not left neglected; second because general affirmation still implies actual tasdiq.
5
Draw the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: The Ash'ari view allows Allah to command what is rationally impossible, while the Hanafi view restricts obligation to what is within capacity, preserving logical consistency.
This principle affects how we understand divine commands that involve future events known to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا المجوّزون التكليف بالمحال ثانياً: وقد كلف الله تعالى أبا جهل بالإيمان، وهو التصديق بجميع ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ومما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن أبا جهل لا يصدق بما جاء به، فقد كلف الله أبا جهل بأن يصدق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في أنه لا يصدقه فيما جاء به، وهو أي تصديقه إنما يكون بانتفاء تصديقه، أي تصديقه في أن لا يصدقه يستلزم أن لا يصدقه؛ إذ لو كان منه التصديق لعلمه؛ لأن كل عاقل يعلم التصديق الواقع منه إذا توجه إليه فلا يعادي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. والجواب عنه: أنه لا تكليف لأبي جهل وأمثاله من العصاة إلا بالتصديق في أحكام الشرع، بأنها من عند الله وبأخباره بالبعث والنشور، والجنة والنار، وعذاب القبر، وعدم التصديق إخبار منه تعالى إلى النبي، وأبو جهل غير مكلف بتصديق هذا الإخبار، فلم يُكلف بتصديق عدم تصديق فلا استحالة، ولا يخرج التصديق الممكن عن الإمكان بعلمه تعالى وإخباره. نقل جوابان آخران والرد عليهما: (١) وما قيل: لو علم أبو جهل يسقط عنه التكليف ممنوع، فإن الإنسان لم يترك سدى. (٢) وقيل في الجواب: إنه مكلف بالتصديق بالجميع إجمالاً، والتصديق بعدم التصديق إنما يستلزم عدم التصديق إذا كان تفصيلاً. أقول: التصديق بالجميع إجمالاً محال منه؛ لأنه يتحقق التصديق منه، وفرض أنه لا تصديق منه، فتدبر. وقوله فتدبر إشارة إلى صعوبة المقام ودقته، وإلى العلم بانطباق الإجمال والتفصيل.
And those who permit impossible obligation said secondly: Allah obligated Abu Jahl to believe, which is affirming all that the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought. And among what the Prophet brought is that Abu Jahl does not affirm what he brought. So Allah obligated Abu Jahl to affirm the Prophet in that he does not affirm him in what he brought. And this (his affirmation) only occurs by the negation of his affirmation, i.e., his affirmation that he does not affirm him necessitates that he does not affirm him, because if he had affirmed, he would have known it, since every rational person knows his own affirmation when he attends to it, so he would not oppose the Prophet. The answer to it: There is no obligation upon Abu Jahl and his like among sinners except affirmation in the rulings of the law, that they are from Allah, and in His reports of the resurrection, paradise, fire, and the punishment of the grave. And the lack of affirmation is a report from Him to the Prophet. Abu Jahl is not obligated to affirm this report, so he was not obligated to affirm the lack of affirmation, so there is no impossibility. And possible affirmation does not leave the realm of possibility by His knowledge and report. Two other answers are narrated and refuted: (1) What was said: If Abu Jahl knew, the obligation would be removed from him – is prevented, for man is not left neglected. (2) It was said in answer: He is obligated to affirm everything in general, and affirming the lack of affirmation only necessitates lack of affirmation if it is in detail. I say: Affirmation of everything in general is impossible from him, because then affirmation would occur from him, and it is assumed that there is no affirmation from him. So ponder. And his saying ‘so ponder’ indicates the difficulty and subtlety of the topic, and indicates knowledge of the applicability of general and detail.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute concerning whether Allah can obligate something impossible (taklif bil-muhal). The opponents (the Ash'ari mu jawwizun) present a second argument: Allah obligated Abu Jahl to believe (tasdiq) in everything brought by the Prophet, yet the Prophet brought that Abu Jahl would not believe. Thus, Abu Jahl must believe that he will not believe, which is self-contradictory and impossible. The Hanafi author refutes this by distinguishing the object of obligation. He states that Abu Jahl is only obligated to affirm the rulings of the law (ahkam al-shar') and the essential reports (akhbar) of the afterlife. The Prophet's report that Abu Jahl will not believe is itself a khabr from Allah, and Abu Jahl is not obligated to affirm that khabr. Therefore, he is not required to affirm his own lack of affirmation, removing the impossibility. The author then critiques two alternative answers: (1) that knowledge of one's disbelief would remove obligation – this is rejected because man is always subject to obligation unless valid excuses exist; (2) that Abu Jahl is obligated to affirm everything generally (ijmalan), and the contradiction only arises in detail (tafsilan) – this is rejected because general affirmation would still entail actual affirmation, contradicting the premise of his disbelief. The passage ends with a comment on the difficulty of the issue. This is a critical refinement of the Hanafi defense against the charge of taklif bil-muhal.

Key Terms
تكليف بالمحال (taklif bil-muhal): Obligating an action that is rationally impossible for the agent to perform.
تصديق (tasdiq): Affirmation or belief in the truth of something.
إجمال (ijmal): General or summary affirmation, as opposed to detailed (tafsil).
تفصيل (tafsil): Detailed specification of the object of belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi response to the Ash'ari argument that Allah obligated Abu Jahl to believe his own non-belief?
A) Abu Jahl was excused due to ignorance.
B) The obligation was only on tasdiq of ahkam al-shar', not on the khabr of his disbelief.
C) Abu Jahl's obligation was removed by his knowledge.
D) Taklif bil-muhal is allowed in some cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school refutes taklif bil-muhal by distinguishing the object of tasdiq, restricting obligation to ahkam al-shar'.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Obligation of Disbelievers with Subsidiary Rulings
Dispute statement on taklif al-kafir bi al-furu'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four positions on taklif al-kafir bi al-furu' and explain the Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute.

Core Idea
Whether a disbeliever is obligated to pray or fast depends on whether belief is a condition for obligation of acts.

A central question in usul al-fiqh is whether a disbeliever is held accountable for performing the subsidiary acts of worship (furu') like prayer and fasting. This lesson presents a multi-school dispute that reveals deep assumptions about the relationship between faith (iman) and action ('amal). We will map the four positions mentioned by the author and understand the practical consequences in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and four positions
Read the Arabic text carefully and list the four views on taklif al-kafir bi al-furu': Shafi'i (obligated with all furu'), Hanafi majority (only obligated to believe), Mu'tazila (also differ), and a fourth view (obligated with prohibitions only).
The text uses 'وقيل' for the fourth view, indicating it is less established.
2
Distinguish between Hanafi groups
Note the internal Hanafi difference: the majority of Hanafis (Bukhara) hold non-obligation, but the Samarkand Hanafis (cited from al-Tahrir) agree with the Shafi'is that disbelievers are obligated with furu'.
This shows that madhhab positions are not monolithic.
3
Analyze the scope of agreement
The text states that in penal and transactional laws, there is agreement under the contract of dhimma. This means once a non-Muslim enters a dhimmi contract, they are bound by Islamic transactional and penal rules.
The dispute is specifically about ritual worship (ibadat), not mu'amalat.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: According to Hanafis (majority), a disbeliever who dies without praying is not punished for missing prayers, because they were never obligated. Shafi'is hold they are punished for both disbelief and missing prayers.
This impacts eschatological punishment but also worldly rulings like converting to Islam and qada' of missed acts.

Issue: Obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings. The disbeliever is obligated with subsidiary rulings according to the Shafi'is, contrary to the Hanafis, and it is said contrary to the Mu'tazila, and it is said with only prohibitions. As for penal and transactional laws, there is agreement under the contract of dhimma. And in al-Tahrir, that is the school of the scholars of Samarkand and those besides them agree on the obligation. 1. The disbeliever is obligated with subsidiary rulings according to the Shafi'is, like prayer and fasting. 2- Contrary to the people of [؟] from the Hanafis, and these are their majority, as al-Isnawi mentioned in Sharh al-Minhaj, and they do not say obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings, rather with belief only as will come. 3- And it is said contrary to the Mu'tazila also in the obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings. 4- And it is said the disbeliever in subsidiary rulings is obligated with only prohibitions, i.e., obligated to avoid prohibitions, so they are punished for committing them only. And as for obligation with penalties
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute (ikhtilaf) on whether a disbeliever is obligated to perform the subsidiary acts of worship (furu') such as prayer and fasting. It enumerates four positions: (1) Shafi'is hold that disbelievers are obligated with all furu', which is also the view of the majority of Hanafis from Samarkand according to al-Tahrir. (2) The Hanafis (specifically the majority of Bukharan Hanafis as per al-Isnawi) hold that disbelievers are only obligated to believe (tasdiq), not to perform acts. (3) The Mu'tazila also hold a contrary view, but it is not detailed. (4) Another view holds that disbelievers are obligated only to avoid prohibitions (manhiyat), meaning they are punished for committing sins but not for leaving obligations. The passage notes agreement that once a dhimmi contract is made, penal and transactional laws apply. This is a statement of the different schools without providing proofs.

Key Terms
تكليف الكافر بالفروع (taklif al-kafir bi al-furu'): Whether a disbeliever is legally obligated to perform subsidiary acts of worship like prayer, fasting, etc.
الفروع (al-furu'): Subsidiary rulings of Islamic law, i.e., practical obligations beyond creed.
عقد الذمة ('aqd al-dhimma): The contract granting protection to non-Muslims in an Islamic state, under which they are bound by penal and transactional laws.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which position is held by the majority of Hanafis (from Bukhara)?
A) Disbelievers are obligated with all furu' acts.
B) Disbelievers are only obligated to believe, not to perform furu'.
C) Disbelievers are obligated with only prohibitions.
D) Disbelievers are obligated with penal laws only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority position is that disbelievers are only obligated to believe, but internal Hanafi disagreement (Samarkand) and cross-madhhab disagreement (Shafi'is) exist, and the practical consequence affects punishment for missed acts of worship.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Taklif al-Kafir bi al-Furu': Obligation of Disbelievers in Subsidiary Rulings
Exposes the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on taklif of a kafir with furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Shafi'i, Samarqandi Hanafi, Iraqi Hanafi, and Bukhari Hanafi positions on taklif al-kafir bi al-furu', and identify the usul principle that underlies the difference.

Core Idea
A kafir's obligation with furu' is disputed because taklif depends on capacity and iman, not just universal address.

This passage presents a key usul dispute: whether a non-Muslim is obligated to perform acts of worship and refrain from prohibitions in the Hereafter. The Shafi'is hold full obligation; the Hanafis are divided. The discussion reveals how regional schools within Hanafism derived different answers from the same principles. We will map the positions and their rationales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
What is taklif al-kafir bi al-furu'? The question is whether a disbeliever is addressed by the commands of shari'a regarding actions like prayer and fasting, such that they are punishable in the Hereafter for abandoning them.
2
Locate the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'is hold that the kafir is fully mukallaf. What is their proof? General address of Quran/Sunnah applies to all humans.
3
Identify the Hanafi positions
The Hanafis are not uniform. First, the scholars of Samarqand (and Bukhara) say the kafir is not obligated with furu' at all. Their basis: iman is a prerequisite for address; dhimma only covers worldly enforcement.
Note the distinction between diyanatan and qada'an.
4
Distinguish among other Hanafis
Other Hanafis (outside Samarqand) agree that the kafir is obligated, but differ: the Iraqis say both belief and performance are required; the Bukharis say only belief is required. Consequence: Iraqis punish for neglecting both; Bukharis punish only for neglecting belief.
This difference is not preserved from Abu Hanifa directly but deduced by later scholars.
5
Note the role of dhimma
For penalties and transactions, there is agreement on worldly enforcement via the dhimma contract, but this does not entail religious obligation (diyanatan). Why does this distinction matter? Because it prevents the conclusion that worldly enforcement implies Hereafter accountability.

Issue: Obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings.

The disbeliever is obligated with subsidiary rulings according to the Shafi'is, contrary to the Hanafis. And it is said contrary to the Mu'tazila. And it is said [obligated] with prohibition only.

As for penalties and transactions, there is agreement with the contract of dhimma.

And in 'al-Tahrir' [it states] that is the madhhab of the scholars of Samarqand, and others besides them agree on the obligation of them.

1. The disbeliever is obligated with subsidiary rulings according to the Shafi'is, like prayer and fasting.
2. Contrary to the Bukharis among the Hanafis, and these are their majority, as mentioned by al-Asnawi in Sharh al-Minhaj, and they do not say that the disbeliever is obligated with subsidiary rulings, rather only with belief as will come. 3. And it is said contrary to the Mu'tazila (also) regarding the obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings. 4. And it is said that the disbeliever in subsidiary matters is obligated with prohibition only, i.e., obligated to act upon prohibitions, meaning they must refrain from prohibitions, so they are punished for committing them only.

As for the obligation of penalties and transactions like hudud, qisas, sales, marriage, and rentals, there is agreement between us and them with the dhimma contract. For the disbeliever being a dhimmi with a covenant and obedient to the people of Islam necessitates that penalties be established upon them and transactions be conducted upon them, just as they are established and conducted upon us. And it does not require that they be obligated religiously (diyanatan) such that they are held accountable in the Hereafter for doing the forbidden and engaging in invalid contracts.

And in 'al-Tahrir' of Ibn al-Humam, that (non-obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings) is the madhhab of the scholars of Samarqand (of the Hanafis). So the scholars of Bukhara and Samarqand agreed on the non-obligation of the disbeliever with subsidiary rulings. 5. And others besides them, besides the scholars of Samarqand, from the Hanafis, agree on the obligation of the disbeliever with them (the subsidiary rulings).

And they differed, i.e., those besides the scholars of Samarqand from the Hanafis, concerning whether the obligation of the disbeliever is regarding performance (performance of subsidiary rulings) like the obligation of believing in subsidiary rulings, meaning just as belief in the obligation of acts of worship and subsidiary rulings is obligatory upon the disbeliever, likewise performing those acts of worship is also obligatory upon him. Or is the obligation of the disbeliever only with respect to belief, without performance and action? The difference is whether the disbeliever is obligated with believing in subsidiary rulings and performing them, or only believing in them?

And they only differed concerning whether regarding performance it is like belief, or only belief? So the Iraqis adopt the first, like the Shafi'is, so they are punished for abandoning both, and the Bukharis adopt the second, so [punished] only for abandoning belief, and it is not preserved from Abu Hanifa and his companions, rather they deduced it.

1. The Iraqis among the Hanafis said: The disbelievers are obligated with the first (belief and performance), like the Shafi'is who say obligation with both belief and performance, so they are punished for abandoning them. 2. The Bukharis among them say they are obligated with the second, i.e., only belief, without performance, so they are punished for abandoning belief only, without abandoning performance. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'only for abandoning belief'.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents a key usul al-fiqh dispute: whether a disbeliever (kafir) is mukallaf (legally obligated) with the furu' al-shari'a (practical rulings such as prayer, fasting, and other acts of worship). The author provides a clear map of positions.

First, the Shafi'i school holds that the kafir is fully obligated with the furu', just like a Muslim. Thus, if a kafir does not pray or fast (even as a disbeliever), they will be punished in the Hereafter for neglecting both the obligation to believe and the obligation to perform. The Hanafis differ.

Among the Hanafis, the passage distinguishes three groups: (1) The scholars of Samarqand, whose view is recorded in Ibn al-Humam's al-Tahrir, say the kafir is not obligated with the furu' at all. (2) The scholars of Bukhara agree with Samarqand on non-obligation. (3) Other Hanafis (outside Samarqand) agree that the kafir is obligated, but they differ as to whether this obligation includes both belief in the obligation of the furu' and actual performance, or only belief.

Specifically, the 'Iraqi' Hanafis (from the school of Kufa) follow the Shafi'i position: obligation includes both belief and performance. Thus the kafir will be punished for abandoning both. The 'Bukhari' Hanafis hold that only belief is obligated; the kafir is punished only for not believing that the furu' are obligatory, not for failing to perform them. The author notes that this latter view is not directly transmitted from Abu Hanifa and his students but is deduced by later scholars.

The passage also addresses a secondary point: for worldly penalties (hudud, qisas) and transactions (sales, marriages, rentals), there is agreement that once a dhimmi contract is established, these laws apply to the disbeliever as they do to Muslims. However, this application is qada'an (judicial/enforcement) not diyanatan (religious accountability). That is, they are enforced in this world by the Islamic state, but this does not entail that the kafir is personally obligated religiously such that they will be punished in the afterlife for violating them (since they may not have believed in their prohibition).

The author carefully traces the intra-Hanafi differences and attributes them to regional scholarly tendencies, showing how usul principles led to divergent positions on the same issue.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklīf): Legal obligation or address of the Lawgiver to a person concerning an action.
كافر (kāfir): One who rejects faith in Allah and the Messenger; a disbeliever.
فروع (furūʿ): Subsidiary or practical rulings of shari'a, i.e., acts of worship and transactions, as opposed to beliefs (usul).
عقد الذمة (ʿaqd al-dhimma): The contract of protection granted to non-Muslims living under Islamic rule, guaranteeing security in exchange for jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars of Samarqand, is a disbeliever obligated to perform the practical acts of worship (furu')?
A) Yes, fully obligated.
B) No, they are not obligated with furu' at all.
C) They are obligated only with beliefs, not actions.
D) They are obligated only with prohibitions.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of a disbeliever with subsidiary rulings is a debated issue in usul al-fiqh, with the Hanafi school divided between those who hold no obligation, those who hold obligation of belief only, and those who hold obligation of both belief and performance.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
The Hanafi Dispute on Obligation of the Disbeliever with Furū‘
Exposition of the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on taklīf of the kāfir with furū‘

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on taklīf al-kāfir bi al-furū‘, explain the evidence for each, and apply the junub analogy to assess the obligation of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Obligation does not require validity: a disbeliever can be bound by Sharia even if his acts are invalid without faith.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: Are disbelievers obligated with the branches of Sharia? The answer is not a simple yes or no — three distinct positions emerge among Hanafis, each rooted in different usul principles. Understanding these positions and their evidence is crucial for correctly applying rulings related to disbelievers in commercial and penal matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi positions
Locate the three groups: Samarkand, Bukhara, and Iraqi Hanafis. For each, note what they say about obligation regarding belief (i‘tiqād) and performance (adā’).
The text explicitly names these groups.
2
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Find the practical example: the case of a vow after apostasy. Determine how each position rules differently.
This shows the real-world impact of the dispute.
3
Analyze the evidence of the negators
Read the three proofs of those who deny obligation (Samarkand/Bukhara). The key argument: if obligation were valid, the act would be valid, but the kafir's act is invalid, so obligation is impossible.
Focus on the logical structure: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
4
Examine the response using the junub analogy
The affirmers counter with the example of a junub (person in major impurity) who is obligated to pray even though his prayer is invalid without purification. Understand how this refutes the negators' first proof.
Obligation can exist even when the act is conditionally invalid.
5
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
Assess the text's tone. It notes that the Bukhari view is deduced (mustanbat), not transmitted directly from Abu Hanifa. Consider which position is more firmly rooted in Hanafi usul.
The text does not explicitly choose, but presents Samarkand and Bukhara as more distinctive to Hanafis.

قلنا في ردّ دليل النافي ( إنه منقوض بالجنب فإنه مكلف بالصلاة حين الجنابة ولا تصح أداء صلوته مع الجنابة (كما لا يصح أداء صلاة الكافر مع الكفر)، فعدم صحة الأداء لا ينافي التكليف ، والحلّ من جانب النافي أنها أي صحة الفروع من الكافر إنما تكون بالشرط وهو الإيمان ، كالمحدث فإنه مكلف بالصلاة بشرط الطهارة، كما يكون
And transactions like Hudud, Qisas, sales, marriage, and leases. So there is agreement between us and them with the contract of dhimma. Indeed, the fact that the disbeliever is a dhimmi with a covenant and obedient to the people of Islam requires that penalties be established on them and transactions be conducted on them, just as they are established and conducted on us. And it does not necessitate that they are obligated (mukallaf) in faith so that they are held accountable in the Hereafter for committing the prohibited and engaging in an invalid contract.
And in Al-Tahrir by Ibn al-Humam, that (non-obligation of the disbeliever with the branches) is the doctrine of the scholars of Samarkand (from the Hanafis). So the scholars of Bukhara and Samarkand agreed on the non-obligation of the disbeliever with the branches. And those other than them, besides the scholars of Samarkand from the Hanafis, are agreed on the obligation of the disbeliever with them (the branches).
And they only differed, i.e., those besides the scholars of Samarkand from the Hanafis, as to whether the obligation of the disbeliever pertains to performance (performance of the branches like the obligation of believing in the branches). That is, just as belief in the obligation of worship and branches is obligatory on the disbeliever, similarly the performance of those worships is also obligatory on him? Or is the obligation of the disbeliever only with respect to belief, without performance and action? The difference is whether the disbeliever is obligated with belief in the branches and their performance, or only with belief?
And they only differed as to whether it is like belief in terms of performance, or only belief. So the Iraqis held the first, like the Shafi'is, so they are punished for abandoning both. The Bukharis held the second, so only on him, and it is not preserved from Abu Hanifa and his companions; rather, they deduced it.
1. The Iraqis from the Hanafis said: The disbelievers are obligated with the first (belief and performance), like the Shafi'is who say they are obligated with both (belief and performance), so they are punished for abandoning them. 2. The Bukharis from them say they are obligated with the second, i.e., only belief, without performance, so they are punished for abandoning belief only, not abandoning performance. This is the meaning of his saying: 'so only on him,' i.e., they are held accountable for abandoning belief only. And this issue (obligation with belief and performance or only belief) is not preserved and transmitted from the early ones (Abu Hanifa and his companions); rather, the Bukharis deduced it from the statement of Muhammad in Al-Mabsut on an issue, which is: someone who vowed to fast a month then apostatized, that fast is not incumbent upon him after he believes, so it is known that disbelief nullifies the obligation of performing worship. And in our Hanafi fiqh, such issues deduced from the statements of the imams are very many.
Evidence of the denier of obligation of the disbeliever with the branches:
First: If it were valid, it would be valid from him, due to compliance with the command, and the consequent is invalid by agreement. We say: It is invalidated by the junub (state of major ritual impurity), and the solution is that it is conditional like the one with minor impurity.
Second: Performance would be possible, but in disbelief it is not possible, and after it there is no command. We say: It is possible at the time of disbelief, even if not possible with the condition of disbelief, and conditional impossibility does not negate intrinsic possibility, and it is invalidated by faith.
Third: Making up (qada') would be obligatory, but it is not obligatory by consensus. We say: The entailment is denied, for Islam wipes out what preceded, so it is as if it is a substitute for all, or it is by a new command.
And the deniers of obligation of the disbeliever with the branches argued with three aspects. Hence the author said:
-1 (The evidence) for the denier: First: That the obligation of the disbeliever with the branches, if valid, those branches would be valid from him, i.e., their performance would be valid from him, because validity is compliance with the command of the Lawgiver, and the consequent (validity of performance from the disbeliever) is invalid by agreement of the deniers and affirmers; for its validity is dependent upon faith.
We say in refutation of the evidence of the denier: It is invalidated by the junub, for he is obligated with prayer during Janabah while his prayer is not valid with Janabah (just as the prayer of the disbeliever is not valid with disbelief). So invalidity of performance does not negate obligation. And the solution from the side of the denier is that validity of the branches from the disbeliever is conditional upon faith, like the one with minor impurity who is obligated with prayer with the condition of purity, just as it is.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul dispute among Hanafis: whether a disbeliever (particularly a dhimmi) is obligated (mukallaf) with the branches (furu‘) of Sharia, meaning both belief in their obligation and actual performance. The author presents three positions:

1. The scholars of Samarkand (a group of Hanafis) held that the kafir is NOT obligated with the branches at all—neither belief nor performance—based on the principle that disbelief nullifies obligation. This is recorded in Ibn al-Humam's Al-Tahrir.

2. The Bukhari Hanafis (another group) held that the kafir is obligated with belief in the branches (i‘tiqad) but not with performance (ada’). They deduced this from a statement of Imam Muhammad in Al-Mabsut: if a Muslim vowed to fast a month then apostatized and later returned to Islam, the vow is not binding after reversion, showing that disbelief removes the obligation of performing acts.

3. The Iraqi Hanafis (like the Shafi‘is) held that the kafir is obligated with both belief and performance, so they are punished for abandoning either.

The author then provides the evidence (dalil) of the negators (those who deny obligation with furu‘) and the responses of the affirmers. The main proof of the negators is that if the kafir were obligated, his acts would be valid (since obligation implies capacity for valid performance), but his acts are invalid due to lack of faith, so obligation is impossible. The response (from the affirmers) uses the analogy of the junub: he is obligated to pray but his prayer is invalid without purification; similarly, the kafir is obligated but his act is conditioned on faith. This shows that invalidity does not negate obligation.

The passage illustrates the usul principle that taklif (obligation) can exist even when performance is not valid due to a missing condition (like faith or purity). The dispute is not directly transmitted from Abu Hanifa but deduced (istinbat) from his usul by later scholars.

Key Terms
تكليف الكافر بالفروع (taklīf al-kāfir bi al-furū‘): Whether a disbeliever is legally obligated to believe in the obligation of the branches of Sharia and/or to perform them.
النافون (al-nāfūn): Those who deny that the kafir is obligated with the branches (i.e., Samarkandi and Bukhari Hanafis in this context).
المثبتون (al-muthbitūn): Those who affirm that the kafir is obligated with the branches (i.e., Iraqi Hanafis and Shafi‘is).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Bukhari Hanafis (as mentioned in the text), what is the kafir obligated with regarding the furu‘?
A) Both belief and performance
B) Only belief, not performance
C) Neither belief nor performance
D) Only performance, not belief

Key Takeaway
The obligation of a disbeliever with the branches of Sharia is a matter of legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf, with three distinct positions based on different usul principles.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Proofs for Taklif al-Kuffar bi al-Furu'
Presenting Quranic proofs for the Hanafi position and refuting ta'wil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on taklif al-kuffar bi al-furu', identify five Quranic proofs for it, and evaluate the opposing ta'wil arguments.

Core Idea
Disbelievers are obligated by Sharia branches; the Quran's general address to 'people' includes them.

A key usul dispute: Are disbelievers obligated to pray, fast, give zakat, etc. before embracing Islam? The Hanafi school says yes, drawing on several Quranic verses that address 'people' generally and that mention punishment for neglecting branches. Opponents use ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to restrict these verses to believers. This lesson unpacks the five proofs and the Hanafi rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the core dispute: Are disbelievers addressed by and obligated to perform the detailed rulings of Sharia (branches)?
This is a well-known usul issue with practical consequences for qada after conversion.
2
List the five Hanafi proofs
Read the five Quranic verses cited. Note the key word 'people' (الناس) and how the verses imply obligation upon all including disbelievers.
Focus on the apparent meaning (zahir) of each verse.
3
Analyze the opposing ta'wil
Understand the opponents' interpretations: e.g., 'prayer' means Islam, 'worship' means faith, 'people' restricted to believers. Identify the mechanism of ta'wil.
The author considers these interpretations far-fetched (بعيد).
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
See why the author rejects the ta'wil: it distorts the general meaning and the Lawgiver's purpose of universal guidance. The plain sense (haqiqah) is preferred without strong evidence for ta'wil.
Hanafi usul holds that amm is binding unless there is a clear takhsis.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does this dispute make? For example, if a disbeliever converts, are past missed prayers required to be made up? Hanafi: no, because the obligation was suspended until faith.
This shows that the dispute has real fiqh consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة مثبتى تكليف الكافر بالفروع
وللمثبت الآيات: (لم نك من المصلين ولم نك نطعم المسكين) أي الزكوة، و(يا أيها الناس اعبدوا ربكم الذي خلقكم)، ولله على الناس حج البيت إلى غير ذلك والتأويل في الكل بعيد.
ودلائل المثبت الآيات الآتية:
-١- قوله تعالى: (كل نفس بما كسبت رهينة * إلا أصحاب اليمين * في جنات يتساءلون * عن المجرمين * ما سلككم في سقر * قالوا لم نك من المصلين * ولم نك نطعم المسكين) أي الزكاة، فعلم أن ترك الصلاة والزكاة سلكهم في النار فهم مكلفون بالفروع.
-٢- وقوله تعالى: (يا أيها الناس اعبدوا ربكم الذي خلقكم) ولفظ الناس عام للكفار والمؤمنين والمنافقين بإيجاد الإيمان والعبادة، وزيادة العبادة وإيجاد الإخلاص في القلب.
-٣- وقوله تعالى: (ولله على الناس حج البيت من استطاع إليه سبيلاً) فلفظ الناس شامل للكافر والمؤمن فوجب الحج على الكفار أيضاً.
-٤- نحو قوله تعالى: (وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين له الدين حنفاء ويقيموا الصلاة ويؤتوا الزكاة) والمخاطب أهل الكتاب والمشركين.
-٥- وقوله تعالى: (وإلى مدين أخاهم شعيباً قال ياقوم اعبدوا الله ما لكم من إله غيره) وكان مخاطب شعيب (عليه السلام) غير المؤمنين.
ثم أشار المصنف إلى ترجيح دلائل المثبتين وقال: "والتأويل في الكل" (جميع الآيات المذكورة) بعيد عن سوق و عموم الآيات، وعن غرض الشارع من هداية الناس جميعاً.
مثال تأويلهم أن يقال:
(١) المراد من نفي الصلاة والزكاة نفي قبول الإسلام من قبيل نفي الشيء بنفي لازمه كناية.
(٢) وأن المراد بالعبادة في الآية الإيمان والمعرفة بذكر اللازم وإرادة الملزوم، وذكر المصنف في تعليقه بعض تأويلاتهم بقوله: مثل حمل (المصلين) على المسلمين، أو عدم أداء الصلاة والزكاة كناية عن عدم الإيمان.
(٣) وأن (الناس) في آية الحج مخصصة بالمسلمين بقرينة قوله تعالى (ومن كفر فإن الله غني عن العالمين) أو المراد بالناس الذين وُجد فيهم شرط وجوب الحج، وهو الإيمان، وهذا شرط قبل شرط استطاعة السبيل.
Proofs of those who affirm the obligation of the disbeliever in the branches: And for the affirmers are the verses: 'We were not of the pray-ers and we were not feeding the poor' i.e. the zakat, 'O people, worship your Lord who created you', and 'To Allah is upon the people the Hajj of the House' and so on, and the interpretation of all is far-fetched. And the proofs of the affirmer are the following verses: 1- His saying, exalted: 'Every soul, for what it has earned, is retained. Except the companions of the right, in gardens they will ask each other about the criminals. What led you into Saqar? They will say, We were not of those who prayed, nor did we feed the poor' i.e. the zakat. So it is known that leaving prayer and zakat led them into the Fire, so they are obligated with the branches. 2- And His saying, exalted: 'O people, worship your Lord who created you' and the word 'people' is general for disbelievers, believers, and hypocrites, to create faith and worship, increase of worship and create sincerity in the heart. 3- And His saying, exalted: 'And to Allah is upon the people the Hajj of the House, whoever is able to find a way to it' so the word 'people' includes the disbeliever and the believer, so Hajj is obligatory upon the disbelievers as well. 4- Like His saying, exalted: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion, inclining to truth, and to establish prayer and give zakat' and the addressees are the People of the Book and the polytheists. 5- And His saying, exalted: 'And to Madyan [We sent] their brother Shu'ayb. He said, O my people, worship Allah; you have no god other than Him.' and the addressees of Shu'ayb (peace be upon him) were other than the believers. Then the author pointed to a preference of the proofs of the affirmers and said: 'And the interpretation of all' (all the mentioned verses) is far from the general meaning and generality of the verses, and from the purpose of the Lawgiver of guiding all people. Example of their interpretation: (1) The intention of negating prayer and zakat is negating acceptance of Islam, from the type of negating a thing by negating its necessary concomitant, as a metonymy. (2) And that the intention of worship in the verse is faith and knowledge, by mentioning the necessary concomitant and intending the accompanied thing, and the author mentioned in his commentary some of their interpretations with his saying: such as carrying 'the pray-ers' to the Muslims, or non-performance of prayer and zakat as a metonymy for non-faith. (3) And that 'the people' in the verse of Hajj is specified to the Muslims by the context of His saying 'And whoever disbelieves, then Allah is free from need of the worlds' or the intention is the people in whom the condition for the obligation of Hajj is found, which is faith, and this is a condition before the condition of capability of the way.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position that disbelievers are obligated by the detailed rulings of Sharia (taklif al-kuffar bi al-furu'). The author, Shaykh Muhammad Anwar al-Badakhshani, lists five Quranic proofs for the affirmers (al-muthbitun). The first verse (al-Muddaththir 74:38-43) shows that the inhabitants of Hell were punished for neglecting prayer and zakat, implying they were obligated. The second (al-Baqarah 2:21) addresses 'people' ('amm) covering all humans. The third (Al 'Imran 3:97) also uses 'people' for Hajj obligation. The fourth (al-Bayyinah 98:5) commands sincerity and worship, addressed to People of the Book and polytheists. The fifth (Hud 11:84) shows Shu'ayb calling his disbelieving people to worship. The author then dismisses the opposing ta'wil (figurative interpretations) as far-fetched, giving examples: (1) negating prayer and zakat is interpreted as negation of Islam; (2) 'worship' is read as faith only; (3) 'people' in Hajj verse is restricted to Muslims. The Hanafi school holds that disbelievers are obligated by both fundamental beliefs (usul) and branches (furu'), though punishment for branch obligations is deferred until faith is accepted. This dispute is a well-known usul issue. The author's tilt is clear: the plain meaning of the texts supports the muthbitun, and the ta'wil is forced.

Key Terms
تكليف الكافر بالفروع (taklīf al-kāfir bi al-furū‘): The legal obligation of a disbeliever to perform the detailed rulings of Sharia (prayer, zakat, etc.) even before embracing Islam.
تأويل (ta’wīl): Figurative interpretation that diverts the apparent meaning of a text to a non-literal sense, here applied to weaken the proofs for taklif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'O people, worship your Lord'
B) 'Every soul, for what it has earned, is retained...'
C) 'And to Allah is upon the people the Hajj'
D) 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah'

Key Takeaway
Disbelievers are obligated by Sharia branches because Quranic general addresses include them, and the Lawgiver's purpose is universal guidance.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Mu'tazila Ta'wil of Quranic Obligations
Mu'tazila ta'wil of three Quranic verses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Mu'tazila ta'wil strategies used to restrict Quranic obligations and evaluate their validity using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila ta'wil distorts general Quranic commands by restricting them to Muslims, contradicting the lawgiver's intent.

This lesson examines how the Mu'tazila interpreted key Quranic verses on prayer, zakat, and Hajj to argue that non-Muslims are not obligated. Their ta'wil uses metaphor and specification to evade the general import. We will parse their reasoning and apply Hanafi usul to critique it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ta'wil strategy
In the source text, the Mu'tazila use three ta'wil: (1) negating prayer/zakat as a metaphor for non-acceptance of Islam; (2) interpreting 'worship' as mere faith; (3) restricting 'people' in Hajj verse to Muslims. Identify each strategy.
Note the use of kinaya (metonymy) in the first example.
2
Analyze the linguistic move
For each ta'wil, determine how they shift the apparent meaning (zahir) to a less apparent one. What linguistic principle are they exploiting?
Hanafi usul distinguishes haqiqah from majaz; Mu'tazila overuse majaz.
3
Assess against Shari'a purpose
The author says these ta'wil are 'far from the market and generality of verses and from the purpose of the Lawgiver in guiding all people.' Evaluate why they fail: do they contradict general commands (amm) and the aim of universal guidance?
Hanafi rule: amm remains general unless there is strong evidence for takhsis.
4
Reconstruct the Hanafi critique
The Hanafi position holds that these verses obligate all people, including non-Muslims, because the commands are general. Provide the counter-argument: negation of prayer does not imply negation of faith; worship includes obedience; Hajj is for all people, with faith as a condition of validity, not obligation.
The condition of faith for Hajj is for the act to be valid, not for the obligation to arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآيات المذكورة بعيد عن سوق و عموم الآيات، وعن غرض الشارع من هداية الناس جميعاً. مثال تأويلهم أن يقال: (1) المراد من نفي الصلاة والزكاة نفي قبول الإسلام من قبيل نفي الشئ بنفي لازمه كناية. (2) وأن المراد بالعبادة في الآية الإيمان والمعرفة بذكر اللازم وإرادة الملزوم. وذكر المصنف في تعليقه بعض تأويلاتهم بقوله: مثل حمل المصلين على المسلمين، أو عدم أداء الصلاة والزكاة كناية عن عدم الإيمان. (3) وأن الناس في آية الحج مخصصة بالمسلمين بقرينة قوله تعالى و من كفر فإن الله غنى عن العالمين أو المراد بالناس الذين وجد فيهم شرط وجوب الحج، وهو الإيمان، وهذا شرط قبل شرط استطاعة السبيل.
The mentioned verses (are) far from the market and the generality of the verses, and from the purpose of the Lawgiver in guiding all people. An example of their ta'wil is to say: (1) The intended meaning of negating prayer and zakat is negating acceptance of Islam, by way of negating the thing by negating its necessary concomitant, as a metaphor. (2) And that the intended meaning of worship in the verse is faith and knowledge, by mentioning the necessary and intending the necessitated. And the author mentioned in his commentary some of their ta'wil by his saying: such as interpreting 'the pray-ers' as Muslims, or non-performance of prayer and zakat as a metaphor for lack of faith. (3) And that 'the people' in the verse of Hajj is specified to Muslims by the context of His saying 'and whoever disbelieves, then Allah is independent of the worlds' or the intended meaning of the people is those in whom the condition of Hajj obligation exists, which is faith, and this is a condition before the condition of having means.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents examples of Mu'tazila's interpretative strategies (ta'wil) used to argue that certain Quranic obligations (prayer, zakat, Hajj) are not binding on non-Muslims. It shows how they manipulate the apparent meanings: first, by taking negations of prayers and zakat as metaphors for non-acceptance of Islam; second, by interpreting 'worship' as mere faith; third, by restricting the addressees of Hajj to Muslims only. The author critiques these by noting they contradict the general import of the verses and the overall purpose of Shari'a.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil): Interpretation that diverts the word from its apparent meaning to a less apparent one, often used by Mu'tazila to support their views.
كناية (kinaya): An indirect expression where the intended meaning is implied through its necessary concomitant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila ta'wil is invalid because it arbitrarily restricts general Quranic commands without the required linguistic or contextual evidence, violating Hanafi usul principles.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Dispute over Continuity of Taklif During the Act
Critical refinement of a disputed usul issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the actual point of dispute between Hanafis and Ash'aris regarding taklif during the act, and apply the Hanafi refutation to the Ash'ari proof and interpretation.

Core Idea
Taklif ceases once the act begins; demanding what already exists is rationally impossible.

This lesson examines a critical usul dispute: does legal obligation (taklif) remain upon the servant during the very moment of performing the commanded act? The passage first corrects a common misattribution to Imam al-Ash'ari, then defines the real disagreement. We will follow the Ash'ari argument and its attempted interpretation, then analyze the Hanafi refutation rooted in the impossibility of demanding the already existing (talab al-mawjud). This dispute is not merely theoretical; it has direct bearing on the nature of gradual acts and the moment of compliance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misattribution
Locate the false claim that al-Ash'ari denied taklif before the act. Understand why the author calls it a necessary error: it would imply no obligation to believe before actually believing and negate the concept of compliance.
The author cites Imam al-Haramayn and al-Amidi to confirm the consensus that taklif exists before the act.
2
Define the real dispute
After clearing the confusion, focus on the actual point of disagreement: whether taklif remains during the occurrence of the act. Note that al-Ash'ari said yes, the Hanafis say no.
The author calls the Ash'ari view false because it is like 'demanding the already existing'.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari interpretation
Examine the Ash'ari attempt to avoid absurdity by claiming taklif attaches to the aggregate of the act's parts. Understand why this fails for instantaneous acts and even for extended acts it leads back to demanding the existing.
The refutation uses two aspects: failure for al-ananiyyat and divisibility of demand.
4
Examine the Ash'ari proof
State the Ash'ari proof: the act is an effect of power, therefore it is within power at the time of existence, so taklif is valid because the only obstacle (incapacity) is removed.
This proof is based on a theological premise about the causal efficacy of created power.
5
Apply the first Hanafi response
The Hanafi refutation begins by denying the premise that the act is an effect of temporal power. Since Ash'aris deny any real causal effect to created power (unlike Mu'tazilites), the proof collapses.
The passage ends here; the other two refutations are not given but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدأ القول في أن التكليف هل هو باق حال حدوث الفعل أم لا؟ وقد نسب إلى الأشعرى أن لا تكليف قبل الفعل، وهذا المذهب منسوب إلى الأشعرى غلط بالضرورة والبداهة، ثم تبيّن على كونه غلطاً وقال: كيف لا تكون هذه النسبة خطأ؟ ويلزم من هذا المذهب أو هذا القول أمران: الأول: أنه على هذا التقدير لا يكون العبد مكلفاً قبل قبول الإيمان وفعله. والثاني: يلزم منه نفي الامتثال، فإنّ الامتثال إنما يتحقق باختيار الفعل ومباشرته بعد العلم بالتكليف، وشعور كونه مكلفاً، ولا تكليف في هذه المسألة قبل الفعل حتى يعلمه. ثم أشار إلى خطأ جماعة من العلماء وقال: ومع ذلك (أي مع هذا الانتساب) تبع الأشعرى جماعة من العلماء، منهم الإمام البيضاوي صاحب "منهاج الوصول إلى علم الأصول"، ولله در الإمام الحرمين حيث قال في كتابه الشامل في الرد على هذا المذهب: هذا مذهب لا يرتضيه عاقل لنفسه. وفي "الإحكام في أصول الأحكام" لسيف الدين الآمدي: التكليف ثابت قبله (قبل الفعل) وينقطع بعده (بعد الفعل) اتفاقاً، أي اتفق العلماء حتى الأشعرى على أن التكليف ثابت قبل الشروع في الفعل، وينقطع بعد انقطاعه، ولا نزاع فيه لأحد، وإنما النزاع في أن التكليف هل هو باق حال حدوث الفعل أم لا؟ قال الأشعرى ببقاء التكليف حال حدوث الفعل، وهو باطل، لأن هذا القول كقولك: الطلب باقي حين وجود المطلوب، وهو خطأ جداً كما ترى. وما يقال في تأويل وتصحيح قول الأشعرى: إن التكليف متعلق بمجموع أجزاء الفعل المأمور به، وهو (المجموع) يحدث ويوجد شيئاً فشيئاً، أي تدريجاً، فيلزم مقارنة التكليف بحدوث الفعل ولا يلزم منه طلب الموجود؛ لأن المجموع إنما يوجد إذا وجد الجزء الأخير، فما لم يوجد الجزء الأخير لم يوجد المجموع. فردّ عليه من وجهين: الأول: أنه لا يتم في الآنيات أي في الأفعال التي تحدث آنًا فآنًا، ولحظة فلحظة. والثاني: إنه فاسد (ولو تم) لأن الفعل الذي كلّف به إذا كان ممتداً كان الطلب المتعلق به محللاً ومنقسماً إلى الأجزاء، فكل جزء منه مسبوق بجزء من الطلب، فيلزم طلب المطلوب الموجود. دليل الأشاعرة على بقاء التكليف حال حدوث الفعل: قالوا في استدلالهم: الفعل المأمور به مقدور تعلق به القدرة حين حدوثه ووجوده، لأنه (ذلك الفعل) أثر القدرة أي حدث بعد تعلق القدرة به، فيصح التكليف به، إذ لا مانع عن التكليف إلا عدم القدرة، وقد انتفى المانع لكونه مقدوراً. وردّ عليه من أوجه ثلاثة: قلنا (أولاً) لا نسلم أن الفعل أثر القدرة؛ فإنه لا تأثير للقدرة الحادثة عندكم (أيها الأشاعرة، نعم لها تأثير عند المعتزلة).
The discussion began whether obligation remains during the occurrence of the act or not. It was attributed to al-Ash‘ari that there is no obligation before the act, and this attribution to al-Ash‘ari is an error by necessity and self-evidence. Then he clarified its being an error and said: How could this attribution not be wrong? Two things follow from this view: First, on this assumption the servant would not be obligated before accepting faith and doing it. Second, it entails denial of compliance, for compliance is only realized by choosing the act and undertaking it after knowledge of the obligation and awareness of being obligated, and there is no obligation in this issue before the act such that he would know it. Then he pointed to the error of a group of scholars and said: Nevertheless (i.e., despite this attribution) a group of scholars followed al-Ash‘ari, among them al-Imam al-Baydawi, author of Minhaj al-Wusul ila ‘Ilm al-Usul. By God, the worth of Imam al-Haramayn, who said in his book al-Shamil in refutation of this view: This is a view that no rational person would approve for himself. And in al-Ihkam fi Usul al-Ahkam by Sayf al-Din al-Amidi: Obligation is established before it (before the act) and ends after it (after the act) by consensus, meaning the scholars, even al-Ash‘ari, agreed that obligation is established before commencing the act and ends after its completion; there is no dispute about it. The dispute is only whether obligation remains during the occurrence of the act or not. Al-Ash‘ari said that obligation remains during the occurrence of the act, and it is false, because this statement is like your saying: The demand remains at the time of the existence of the demanded, which is a serious error as you see. And what is said in interpretation and correction of al-Ash‘ari's statement: that obligation pertains to the total of the parts of the commanded act, and the total comes into existence gradually, so it entails the coincidence of obligation with the occurrence of the act and does not entail demanding the already existing because the total only exists when the last part exists; as long as the last part has not existed, the total has not existed. It was refuted from two aspects: First, it does not apply to instantaneous acts, i.e., acts that occur moment by moment and instant by instant. Second, it is invalid (even if it did apply) because if the commanded act is extended, the demand pertaining to it is analyzable and divisible into parts, so each part of the act is preceded by a part of the demand, entailing demanding the already existing. The proof of the Ash‘aris for the continuation of obligation during the act: They said in their argument: The commanded act is within power, power attaches to it at the time of its occurrence and existence, because the act is an effect of power, i.e., it occurs after power attaches to it, so obligation regarding it is valid, for there is no obstacle to obligation except lack of power, and the obstacle is removed because it is within power. It was refuted from three aspects: We say (firstly): We do not concede that the act is an effect of power, for there is no effect of temporal power according to you (the Ash‘aris); rather, it has an effect according to the Mu‘tazilites.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from a Hanafi usul commentary. It begins by correcting a misattribution: the view that there is no taklif before the act is wrongly ascribed to Imam al-Ash'ari. The author shows this is self-evidently false, because it would mean no obligation to believe before actually believing, and it would negate the very meaning of compliance (imtithal), which requires prior knowledge of the obligation. He then praises Imam al-Haramayn for dismissing such a view as irrational. Next, he cites al-Amidi to define the actual point of disagreement: everyone agrees taklif exists before the act and ends after it; the dispute is only whether taklif continues during the very moment of performing the act. Al-Ash'ari held that it does, but the author calls it false because it is like demanding something already being brought into existence. An attempted interpretation by some Ash'aris—that taklif attaches to the aggregate of the act's parts which come into being gradually—is refuted: it fails for instantaneous acts, and even for extended acts it leads again to demanding the already existing, since each part of the act is preceded by a part of the demand. Then the Ash'ari proof is presented: the act is an effect of power, hence it is within power, so taklif is valid. The Hanafi response first denies the premise that the act is an effect of power, because the Ash'ari school denies any real causal efficacy to created power (only the Mu'tazilites affirm it). The passage ends mid-refutation, only giving the first of three counter-arguments. The Hanafi position is that taklif does not continue during the act; once the act begins, the command is fulfilled and ceases. This dispute flows from deeper theological differences about power and causation.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif): الطلب من العبد ما فيه كلفة، سواء كان إيجاباً أو تحريماً، والمقصود هنا التكليف بالشرع (Legal obligation imposing a demand on the servant, whether command or prohibition; here specifically the shar'i obligation)
طلب الموجود (talab al-mawjud): تصور طلب الشيء الذي هو كائن بالفعل، وهو مستحيل عقلاً لأنه تحصيل حاصل (Demanding something that already exists; it is rationally impossible because it is the acquisition of what has already been acquired)
الآنيات (al-ananiyyat): الأفعال التي تحدث في آنات متقطعة، مثل الحركات المتعاقبة التي لا امتداد لها (Acts that occur in discrete instants, such as successive movements that have no duration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the actual point of disagreement between the Hanafis and the Ash'aris regarding taklif?
A) Whether taklif exists before the act or only after it.
B) Whether taklif remains during the occurrence of the act or ends before it.
C) Whether taklif applies to instantaneous acts or only to extended acts.
D) Whether taklif is conditional upon knowledge of the obligation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that taklif does not continue during the act; to claim otherwise entails the rationally impossible demand for what already exists.
