Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Persistence of Taklif During the Act: Hanafi Refutation of the Ash'ari View
Refutes Ash'ari view that taklif remains during the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether taklif remains during an act and evaluate the Ash'ari counterargument by identifying the three Hanafi refutations.

Core Idea
Taklif cannot persist during the act because demanding what already exists is rationally impossible.

All scholars, including al-Ash'ari, agree that taklif is established before the act and ends after it. The only dispute is whether it persists during the act itself. The Ash'ari view that it does persist is rejected by the Hanafi-Maturidi school as rationally impossible, because it would entail demanding something that is already occurring, i.e., talab al-mawjud. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of the dispute, examining the Ash'ari proofs and the precise Hanafi responses grounded in the nature of capability (qudra) and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of agreement
First, note that all scholars agree that taklif exists before an act begins and ceases after it ends. The disagreement is confined to the period during the act.
2
State the Ash'ari position and its attempted salvage
Al-Ash'ari held that taklif remains during the act. His followers attempted to defend this by claiming that taklif attaches to the totality of the act's parts, so it only ends when the last part is completed. Understand why this fails for both instantaneous and extended acts.
The author refutes this salvage with two arguments: one for instantaneous acts (where the whole act exists at once) and one for extended acts (where the demand would be divided).
3
Analyze the Ash'ari proof from capability
The Ash'arites argue that the commanded act is within the agent's capability (maqdur) at the moment of occurrence because it is an effect of qudra. Since the only impediment to obligation is lack of capability, the obligation can persist.
This proof assumes that capability has a causal effect and that the act is contingent upon it.
4
Work through the three Hanafi refutations
The Hanafi response dismantles the Ash'ari proof step by step: (1) Created capability has no causal effect according to Ash'arites themselves, so the act is not its effect. (2) Even if it were, the act becomes necessary once chosen, and the necessary is beyond capability. (3) Even if the act were within capability, demanding what already exists remains an independent impediment.
Each refutation targets a different premise of the Ash'ari argument, culminating in the overarching absurdity of talab al-mawjud.
5
Synthesize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute affects whether a person is considered obedient/disobedient during the performance of the act. According to the Hanafi view, obedience is only before and after, while the Ash'ari view would allow it during. This has implications for continuous acts like prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشامل في الرد على هذا المذهب : هذا مذهب لا يرتضيه عاقل لنفسه . تعيين محل النزاع وفي "الإحكام في أصول الأحكام لسيف الدين الأمدى: التكليف ثابت قبله قبل الفعل) و ينقطع بعده (بعد الفعل) اتفاقا، أي اتفق العلماء حتى الأشعرى على أن التكليف ثابت قبل الشروع في الفعل، وينقطع بعد انقطاعه، ولانزاع فيه لأحد، وإنما النزاع في أن التكليف هل هو باق حال حدوث الفعل أم لا ؟ قال الأشعرى ببقاء التكليف حال حدوث الفعل، وهو باطل ، لأن هذا القول كقولك : الطلب باقي حين وجود المطلوب وهو (خطأ جداً) كما ترى. ذكر تأويل قول الأشعرى ثم الرد عليه وما يقال في تأويل و تصحيح قول الأشعرى : إن التكليف متعلق بمجوع أجزاء الفعل المأمور به ، وهو ( المجموع) يحدث ويجد شيئاً فشيئاً ، أي تدريجاً، فيلزم مقارنة التكليف بحدوث الفعل ولا يلزم منه طلب الموجود؛ لأن المجموع إنما يوجد إذا وجد الجزء الأخير ، فمالم يوجد الجزء الأخير لم يوجد المجموع. فردّ عليه من وجهين : الأول: أنه لا يتم في الآنيات أي في الأفعال التي تحدث آناً فآناً، ولحظة فلحظة. والثانى: إنه فاسد (ولوتم) لأن الفعل الذى كلّف به إذا كان ممتداً كان الطلب المتعلق به محللاً و منقسماً إلى الأجزاء، فكل جزء منه مسبوق بجزء من الطلب . فيلزم طلب المطلوب الموجود. دليل الأشاعرة على بقاء التكليف حال حدوث الفعل قالوا في استدلالهم : الفعل المأمور به مقدور تعلق به القدرة حين حدوثه ووجوده ، لأنه (ذلك الفعل) أثر القدرة أي حدث بعد تعلق القدرة به ، فيصح التكليف به ، إذ لا مانع عن التكليف إلا عدم القدرة ، وقد انتفى المانع لكونه مقدوراً. وردّ عليه من أوجه ثلاثة: قلنا (أولاً) لانسلّم أن الفعل أثر القدرة : فإنه لا تأثير للقدرة الحادثة عندكم (اتها الأشاعرة ، نعم لها تأثير عند المعتزلة ( وثانياً) لو سلّم أن الفعل أثر القدرة فلا نسلم أنه (كون الفعل أثراً للقدرة) يستلزم المقدورية، أي كون الفعل مقدوراً ، فإنّه (أي الفعل ) يجب و يصير وجوده ضرورياً باختيار الفاعل إيَّاه ، لأن الشيئ ( فعلاً كان أو غيره) مالم يجب، ولم يصر وجوده ضرورياً لم يوجد ، فإذا وُجِدَ الفعل يكون واجباً، والواجب لا يكون مقدوراً فلا يختاره ولا يقدر عليه فإنّه فوق اختياره. ( وثالثاً) لو سلّم كون الفعل أثراً للقدرة واستلزامه المقدورية، فلانسلّم أن كل مقدور يصح التكليف به ولامانع من صحة التكليف به، إلا ذلك (عدم القدرة به). بل هنا مانع آخر، وهو لزوم طلب الموجود، هذا هو قبح بقاء التكليف حال حدوث الفعل.
The all-encompassing in refutation of this doctrine: This is a doctrine that no rational person would accept for himself. Determining the point of dispute: In al-Ihkam fi Usul al-Ahkam by Sayf al-Din al-Amidi: 'Taklif is established before the act (before the action) and ends after it (after the action) by agreement.' That is, the scholars, even al-Ash'ari, agreed that taklif is established before commencing the action and ends after its cessation, and there is no dispute about that. The dispute is only whether taklif remains during the occurrence of the action or not. Al-Ash'ari said that taklif remains during the occurrence of the action, and this is false, because this statement is like saying: 'The demand remains at the time of the existence of the demanded,' which is (very wrong) as you see. Mention of the interpretation of al-Ash'ari's statement and then the refutation of it. And what is said in interpretation and correction of al-Ash'ari's statement: that taklif is related to the totality of the parts of the commanded action, and the totality occurs and comes into existence gradually, so it necessitates the apposition of taklif with the occurrence of the action and does not necessitate demanding the already existent, because the totality only exists when the last part exists; so as long as the last part has not existed, the totality has not existed. This is refuted in two ways: First: It does not hold for instantaneous actions, i.e., actions that occur moment by moment and instant by instant. Second: It is corrupt even if it were to hold, because if the commanded action is extended, the demand related to it is divisible and divided into parts, so each part is preceded by a part of the demand. Thus it necessitates demanding what already exists. The evidence of the Ash'arites for the persistence of taklif during the occurrence of the action: They said in their argument: The commanded action is within capability, and capability is attached to it at the time of its occurrence and existence, because that action is an effect of capability, i.e., it occurs after capability is attached to it. So it is valid to obligate it, since there is no impediment to obligation except lack of capability, and the impediment has been removed because it is within capability. It is refuted from three aspects: We say: (First) We do not accept that the action is an effect of capability, for the originated capability has no effect according to you (the Ash'arites); yes, it has effect according to the Mu'tazilites. (Second) Even if we accept that the action is an effect of capability, we do not accept that (the action being an effect of capability) entails being within capability, i.e., the action being within capability. Because the action (becomes necessary) and its existence becomes necessary by the agent's choice. For a thing (whether action or otherwise) as long as it is not necessary and its existence has not become necessary, it does not exist. So when the action exists, it is necessary, and what is necessary cannot be within capability; it is not chosen nor can it be acted upon, for it is beyond choice. (Third) Even if we accept that the action is an effect of capability and that entails being within capability, we do not accept that everything within capability is valid to be obligated and there is no impediment to the validity of obligating it except that (lack of capability). Rather, there is another impediment here, which is the necessity of demanding what already exists. That is the repugnance of the persistence of taklif during the occurrence of the action.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute between the Hanafi-Maturidi school and the Ash'ari school regarding the persistence of taklif (legal obligation) during the actual performance of an act. The author begins by clarifying the point of agreement: all scholars, including al-Ash'ari, agree that taklif is established before the act and ends after the act. The dispute is solely about whether it continues during the act. Al-Ash'ari's positive view is dismissed as repugnant because it would mean demanding what is already being done, which is rationally impossible. The author then addresses an attempted salvage of al-Ash'ari's position by positing that taklif attaches to the totality of parts, which only fully exists at the end. This is refuted first for instantaneous acts (where the whole act is present at once) and second for extended acts, because the demand would be divided among parts, still requiring the impossible. The Ash'arite proof—that the act is already within the agent's power (maqdur) so there is no impediment—is countered in three steps: (1) Created power has no effect according to Ash'arites (who deny causal efficacy to creatures) but only with Mu'tazilites; (2) Even if it did, the act's existence becomes necessary once chosen, and what is necessary is not within power (a ta'liq on Ash'ari occasionalism); (3) Even if the act were within power, there remains the separate impediment of demanding the already existent. The reliance on rational argument (dalil aqli) is characteristic of this later Hanafi commentary, which engages theological usul debates.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklif): Legal obligation (the divine command to perform an action)
القدرة (al-qudra): Capability or power to perform an action
المأمور به (al-ma'mur bihi): The commanded action (the object of obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because no action can be within capability at the moment of its occurrence.
B) Because it entails demanding what already exists.
C) Because created capability has no effect according to Maturidis.
D) Because taklif only applies to instantaneous actions.

Key Takeaway
Taklif cannot persist during the act because demanding what already exists is rationally impossible, a principle affirmed by the Hanafi school.

Dispute on Timing of Qudra: Maturidi vs Ash'ari
Compares Maturidi and Ash'ari views on qudra timing for taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Maturidi-Hanafi position on the timing of qudra relative to the act and evaluate the Ash'ari counter-argument using the critique of the analogy of divine qudra.

Core Idea
Qudra must precede the act, for a condition cannot be simultaneous with its conditioned — otherwise the kafir would never be obligated to believe.

This passage delves into a foundational usul dispute: the timing of capacity (qudra) as a condition for legal obligation (taklif). Both Maturidi-Hanafis and Ash'arites agree qudra is necessary, but they diverge on whether it exists before the act or simultaneously. The Hanafi position is rooted in the logic that conditions precede their conditioned, while the Ash'ari view relies on the inseparability of power from its object. Understanding this debate clarifies the basis for obligating the disbeliever before he believes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and agreed premise
Both schools agree that qudra is a condition (shart) for taklif. The dispute is whether qudra exists before the act or simultaneously with it.
This agreement is important; the disagreement is only about timing.
2
State the Maturidi-Hanafi position and its first proof
Qudra must be before the act because a condition (shart) must precede its conditioned (mashrut). This is a rational necessity.
The first argument is deductive: shart precedes mashrut.
3
Present the second proof regarding the kafir
If qudra were simultaneous, the kafir would lack qudra before the act of faith, so he could not be obligated to believe before believing, which is absurd. Therefore qudra must precede.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against the Ash'ari position.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari rejoinder and the analogy of striking
The Ash'arites argue that qudra's relation to its object (maqdur) is like striking to the struck—inseparable. Thus qudra must be simultaneous.
This is an analogy (qiyas) used to support simultaneity.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal using divine qudra
The Hanafis respond that if the analogy held, then God's qudra would always have an object, implying the world's eternity. Instead, qudra has the capacity (salahiyya) for relation, not actual relation. Thus the analogy fails.
The rebuttal uses a counterexample from divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الاختلاف بين الماتريدية والأشاعرة في كون القدرة قبل الفعل أو معه؟ القدرة شرط التكليف اتفاقاً، لكن قبل الفعل عندنا وعند المعتزلة ومعه عند الأشعرية لنا أولاً أنها شرط الفعل اختياراً، وهو قبل المشروط تدبر وثانياً لوكانت معه لزم عدم كون الكافر مكلفاً بالإيمان قبله؛ لأنه غير مقدور له في تلك الحالة، وأجيب شرط التكليف عندنا أن يكون هو متعلقا للقدرة أو ضده كذا في المواقف أقول: ليس كخلق الجواهر اتفاقاً، بل الكافر عندنا كالساكن و عندهم كالمقيد لا بل عندنا كالمقيد، وعندهم كالزمن، والتفرقة ضرورية، وإنكارهم مكابرة قالوا :أولاً: إنها متعلقة بالمقدور تعلق الضرب بالمضروب ووجود المتعلّق بدون المتعلّق محال قلنا : منقوض بقدرة البارى تعالى، وإلا لزم قدم العالم، بل هي لها صلاحية التعلق
Issue: The disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'arites regarding whether capacity (qudra) is before the action or with it. Capacity is a condition of taklif by agreement. However, before the action according to us and the Mu'tazilites, and with it according to the Ash'arites. For us: First, it is a condition of the action by choice, and it is before the conditioned, reflect. Second, if it were with it, it would necessitate that the disbeliever is not obligated to faith before it, because it is not within his capability at that moment. It was answered: The condition of taklif according to us is that he is a locus for capability or its opposite, as in al-Mawaqif. I say: It is not like the creation of essences by agreement. Rather, the disbeliever is according to us like the stationary, and according to them like the chained. No, rather according to us like the chained, and according to them like the paralyzed. The distinction is necessary, and their denial is stubbornness. They said: First: It (capacity) is related to the capable as striking is related to the struck, and the existence of the related without the relat [؟] is impossible. We say: This is refuted by the capability of the Exalted Creator; otherwise the eternity of the world would be necessitated. Rather, it has the capacity of relation.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute between the Hanafi-Maturidi school and the Ash'ari school concerning the temporal relation of qudra (capacity/power) to the act of the mukallaf (obligated person). Both schools agree that qudra is a condition (shart) for taklif. The point of contention is whether qudra exists before the act (as Maturidis and Mu'tazilites hold) or simultaneously with the act (as Ash'arites hold). The Hanafi position gives two arguments: first, qudra is a condition for voluntary action, and a condition must precede its conditioned; second, if qudra were simultaneous, then before the act the kafir would not have qudra at that moment, so he could not be obligated to believe before actually believing, which is absurd. The Ash'arite response (from al-Mawaqif) is that the condition for taklif is simply that the person is a locus (mahall) for qudra or its opposite (i.e., he has the potential for it). The author then distinguishes between the kafir (disbeliever) and other cases: The kafir is likened to a stationary person (who can move) according to us, but to a chained person according to them; then a correction: according to us he is like the chained (limited but not impossible), and according to them like the paralyzed (completely incapable). This is a subtle qiyas. The Ash'arite further argue that qudra's relation to the maqdur is like striking to the struck—the two cannot be separated. The Hanafi rebuttal cites the divine qudra: if relation were inseparable, it would imply the world's eternity (since God's qudra must always have had an object), which is false. Instead, qudra has the capacity (salahiyya) for relation, not actual relation. This passage illustrates the interplay of rational theology (kalam) and usul al-fiqh in the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): Capacity/power to perform an action, a condition for taklif.
الكافر (al-kafir): Disbeliever, used as a test case for the obligation of faith before actual belief.
شرط التكليف (shart al-taklif): Condition for legal obligation, here identified as being a locus for qudra or its opposite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi-Hanafi position in this passage, why must qudra (capacity) be before the act?
A) Because otherwise the kafir would not be obligated to believe at any moment.
B) Because qudra is a condition for action by choice, and a condition must precede its conditioned.
C) Because the Ash'arite position implies the eternity of the world.
D) Because qudra cannot relate to a nonexistent object.

Key Takeaway
Qudra as a condition for taklif must precede the act; potential qudra suffices, and the Hanafi-Maturidi position is grounded in rational arguments about the nature of conditions and the critique of the Ash'ari analogy.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Timing of Capacity: Maturidi vs Ash'ari
Usul dispute on timing of capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi (Maturidi) position on the timing of capacity relative to the voluntary act, explain the two Maturidi proofs, and evaluate the Ash'ari objections and Hanafi responses.

Core Idea
Capacity must precede the act – otherwise, a disbeliever could not be obligated to believe.

In usul al-fiqh, capacity (qudra) is a condition for moral obligation (taklif) by consensus, but its timing relative to the voluntary act is disputed. The Hanafi school, following the Maturidi theological tradition, holds that capacity exists before the act, while the Ash'aris argue it coexists with the act. This disagreement has direct implications for the obligation of a disbeliever to embrace faith and for the very possibility of divine command. This lesson unpacks the proofs and counter-proofs from this passage, strengthening your ability to analyse theological-legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognise that the dispute is about the timing of capacity (qudra) — a condition for taklif — relative to the voluntary act: before or with it?
Both schools agree capacity is a condition; disagreement is on timing.
2
State the Hanafi position and its two proofs
First, capacity is a condition for voluntary action; conditions precede the conditioned. Second, if capacity were with the act, a disbeliever would not be obligated to believe before believing, because faith would not be within his capacity at that moment.
These proofs are based on rational necessity (dalil aqli).
3
Understand the Ash'ari objections
Ash'aris argue: (1) capacity relates to the possible as hitting relates to the hit; the related cannot exist without the related. (2) capacity is an accident that does not persist, so if it preceded it would vanish. (3) the act cannot occur before capacity, so it would not be possible.
These objections challenge the logical coherence of preceding capacity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi responses
Hanafis respond: (1) the objection is refuted by Allah's capacity (otherwise the world would be eternal). (2) even if accidents do not persist, the condition is the universal nature that persists through succession. (3) the act is possible in the sense of being valid for relation, not actual before capacity.
Each response defends the coherence of precedence.
5
Recognise the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: whether a disbeliever is obligated to believe before actually believing, and whether taklif can apply to something before capacity exists.
This affects our understanding of divine justice and human responsibility.

(Secondly) If it is granted that the act is an effect of capacity, we do not grant that (the act being an effect of capacity) entails being possible (maqdur), i.e., the act being possible. For the act becomes necessary and its existence becomes inevitable by the agent's choice of it, because a thing (whether act or otherwise) as long as it is not necessary and its existence has not become inevitable, does not exist. So when the act exists, it is necessary, and the necessary is not possible; thus it is neither chosen nor within capacity, for it is beyond choice. (Thirdly) If it is granted that the act is an effect of capacity and that this entails being possible, we do not grant that every possible thing is valid for moral obligation and that there is no impediment to the validity of moral obligation except that (lack of capacity). Rather, here there is another impediment, which is the necessity of demanding something that exists; this is the repugnance of the continuation of moral obligation at the moment of the act's occurrence.
:6 Issue: The disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris concerning whether capacity is before the act or with it. Capacity is a condition of moral obligation by consensus, but it is before the act according to us and the Mu'tazilites, and with it according to the Ash'aris.
We have, first: that it is a condition for voluntary action, and it is before the conditioned, so reflect. Second: if it were with it, it would necessitate that the disbeliever is not obligated for faith before it, because it is not within his capacity in that state. It was answered: the condition of moral obligation according to us is that it (the act) be capable of being related to capacity or its opposite, as in al-Mawaqif.
I say: It is not like the creation of atoms by consensus. Rather, the disbeliever according to us is like a stationary person, and according to them like a restrained person. No, rather according to us he is like a restrained person, and according to them like a paralyzed person. The distinction is necessary, and their denial is obstinacy.
They said: First: It (capacity) is related to the possible as the hitting relates to the hit, and the existence of the related without the related is impossible.
We said: It is refuted by the capacity of the Exalted Creator, otherwise the eternity of the world would follow. Rather, it has the fitness for relation.
Second: It is an accident, and it does not persist for two moments. So if it preceded, it would cease to exist, and thus it would not relate (to anything).
We said: Even if non-persistence is granted, the condition is the universal nature that persists by the succession of similar instances.
Third: The act cannot occur before it (capacity), so it is not possible, and this is as you see.
He said: Capacity is a condition of moral obligation by consensus between us and the Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites, (1) but it is before the act according to us and the Mu'tazilites, (2) and with it (with the act) according to the Ash'aris.
And the meaning of condition is that the conditioned does not exist without it, nor does its existence necessitate the existence of the conditioned. Here there is a problem: that the author has previously stated that moral obligation of the impossible is permissible according to the Ash'ari, so there is no meaning for capacity being a condition for moral obligation of the impossible which the servant has no capacity for. So how did the Ash'ari say that capacity is a condition for moral obligation absolutely? It is said in response: Perhaps the author did not consider this attribution to the Ash'ari, i.e., the attribution of the permissibility of moral obligation of the impossible to al-Ash'ari.
Proofs of the Maturidis that capacity is before the act.
And the author mentioned two proofs from their side: He said: The proof for us: First: That it (capacity) is a condition for voluntary action, i.e., a condition for the voluntary act in that the obligated person chooses the act after its existence, and the condition is before the conditioned, so capacity is before the act.
And he indicated in the commentary on explaining his statement "reflect" (tadabbar) and said: You may say: The condition for voluntary action is the validity of the act's relation to capacity, not capacity itself. There is no doubt that the act being such that it is valid to be related to capacity precedes the act. That is, if the condition of the act is the validity of its relation to capacity, and that is prior to the act, then capacity itself being a condition is prohibited.
And the second proof for us: (That) if it (capacity) were with it (the act), it would necessitate that the disbeliever is not obligated for faith before it, before accepting faith (because faith is not within his capacity in that state (the state of disbelief), for capacity is with the act according to them, and there is no act of faith.)
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul al-fiqh discussion on the dispute between the Maturidi school (which Hanafis follow) and the Ash'ari school regarding the timing of capacity (qudra) relative to the voluntary act. The core issue is whether capacity exists before the act (Maturidi and Mu'tazili view) or simultaneously with it (Ash'ari view). The text first presents additional arguments against the idea that the act being an effect of capacity entails that the act is within one's power (maqdur). It then formally states the disagreement and gives two proofs for the Maturidi position: (1) Capacity is a condition for voluntary action, and a condition must precede its conditioned; (2) If capacity were with the act, then a disbeliever would not be obligated to believe before actually believing, since capacity for faith would only arise at the moment of belief. The text also addresses Ash'ari objections about the nature of capacity, its relation to the possible, its accidental nature, and the impossibility of the act without preceding capacity. It concludes with the Maturidi proofs and a comment that the condition for voluntary action is actually the validity of relation to capacity, not capacity itself. The commentary emphasizes that this debate directly affects the validity of moral obligation (taklif) and the nature of human agency in Islamic theology.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): Capacity or power that enables an agent to perform a voluntary act, considered a condition for moral obligation.
شرط التكليف (shart al-taklif): A condition that must be present for moral obligation to be valid, here specifically capacity.
الفعل الاختياري (al-fi'l al-ikhtiyari): Voluntary action performed by choice, requiring capacity for its occurrence.
التكليف بالممتنع (al-taklif bi al-mumtani'): Moral obligation of the impossible, a point of contention attributed to the Ash'ari school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) If capacity were with the act, a disbeliever would not be obligated for faith before believing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi (Maturidi) position that capacity precedes the act is supported by the principle that conditions precede conditioned and by the implication for the disbeliever's obligation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
15 min
Capability as a Condition for Taklif: Maturidi vs. Ash'ari
Theological-legal dispute on capability as a condition for obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi-Maturidi position on capability as a condition for taklif and evaluate the Ash'ari counterargument using rational proofs.

Core Idea
Capability must precede the act—otherwise obligation and sin become meaningless.

This passage delves into a foundational theological dispute between the Maturidi (Hanafi) and Ash'ari schools regarding the role of human capability (qudra) in legal obligation (taklif). While both agree that capability is a necessary condition, they differ on whether it must exist before the act or simultaneously with it. The author presents two rational proofs for the Maturidi position, responds to an Ash'ari objection, and reframes the analogy to show the weakness of the Ash'ari view. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi stance on human responsibility and the basis for sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Dispute
State the issue: whether capability (qudra) is a condition for taklif before the act or simultaneous with it. Note the agreement between Maturidis, Ash'aris, and Mu'tazilites that it is a condition, but the disagreement on timing.
The condition is defined as 'that without which the conditioned does not exist, but whose existence does not necessitate the conditioned.'
2
Analyze the First Maturidi Proof (Condition-Conditioned Relation)
The first proof argues that capability is a condition for the voluntary act, and a condition logically precedes its conditioned. Thus capability must be before the act. The author addresses an objection that the real condition is the 'validity of attachment' to capability, not capability itself.
The objection is mentioned in the commentary: 'You may say: the condition for the voluntary act is the validity of the act's attachment to capability, not capability itself.' The author implies this is a subtlety but does not refute it here.
3
Analyze the Second Maturidi Proof (Consequence for Disbeliever)
The second proof shows an absurd consequence: if capability were simultaneous with the act, then a disbeliever would not be obligated to faith before embracing it (since no act of faith occurs). This would mean a disbeliever dying in disbelief is not sinful, which is absurd.
Sin is a branch of taklif; without valid taklif, no sin.
4
Examine the Ash'ari Response and Maturidi Refutation
The Ash'aris respond that the condition is that the act or its opposite be capable—so a disbeliever is capable of disbelief, fulfilling the condition for obligation of faith. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the impossible (like creating atoms) and the possible but prevented (like faith hampered by obstinacy).
The author uses the analogy: the disbeliever is like a tied person (capable but prevented), while the Ash'ari view makes him like a lame person (inherently incapable).
5
Extract the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The practical difference: On the Maturidi view, a disbeliever is sinful for not using prior capability; on the Ash'ari view, he is not sinful because obligation was impossible. This affects rulings on the fate of disbelievers and the basis of divine justice.
The author sides decisively with the Maturidis, calling the Ash'ari denial 'clear obstinacy'.

عندهم (الأشاعرة ( كالمقيد الذى لا يقدر على الحركة، وإن أراد؛ لأنه يمنعه القيد عن الحركة. وبما أن الأشاعرة قالوا: إن الكافر حال الكفر ليس بقادر على الإيمان أصلاً، فحاله ليس كحال المقيد، أعرض المصنف عن مثاله الأول، وجاء بمثال آخر ، وقال "لا" أي ليس الكافر عندنا كالساكن، وعندهم كالمقيد، بل هو عندنا كالمقيد ، وعندهم كالزمن ( الأعرج) لأن الكافر عندنا قادر على الإيمان ، لكن الكفر ورسوخ العقائد الباطلة ، منعه عن الإيمان ، كالمقيد القادر على الحركة، ولكن القيد يمنعه عن الحركة، وأما كونه عندهم كالزمن (الأعرج ) فلأن الكافر ليس بقادر على الإيمان عندهم أصلاً كالزمن الذي لا يقدر على الحركة والمشى أصلاً، And secondly: that it (capability) is an accident, and it does not remain for two time-instants. So if it preceded, it would cease to exist, thus it would not be attached to something. We say: even if we concede non-endurance, the condition is the universal nature which remains by successive instances. And thirdly: the act cannot occur before it, so it would not be within capability, and that is as you see. He said: Capability is a condition for taklif (legal obligation) by agreement among us, the Ash'aris, and the Mu'tazilites. However, it is before the act according to us and the Mu'tazilites, and simultaneous (with the act) according to the Ash'aris. The meaning of 'condition' is that the conditioned does not exist without it, and its existence does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. Here there is an issue: the author has previously stated that obligation of the impossible is permissible according to the Ash'ari, so there is no meaning for capability being a condition for obligation of the impossible which the slave has no capability for. So how did the Ash'ari state the conditionality of capability for obligation absolutely? It is said in response: Perhaps the author did not consider this attribution to the Ash'ari, i.e., attributing the permissibility of obligating the impossible to the Ash'ari. Proofs of the Maturidis that capability is before the act. And the author mentioned from their side two proofs: He said: The proof for us: First: that it (capability) is a condition for the voluntary act, i.e., a condition for the voluntary act such that the obligatee chooses the act after its existence, and it (the condition) is prior to the conditioned, thus capability is before the act. And he indicated in the commentary to explain his statement: (reflect) and said: You may say: the condition for the voluntary act is the validity of the act's attachment to capability, not capability itself. And there is no doubt that the act being such that it can be attached to capability is prior to the act. So if the condition for the act is the validity of its attachment to capability, and that is prior to the act, then capability itself being a condition is prohibited. And the second proof for us: that if it (capability) were simultaneous (if capability were with the act), then it would necessitate that the disbeliever is not obligated to believe before it, before he accepts faith, because faith is not within his capability in that state (state of disbelief), since according to them capability is with the act, and there is no act of faith. And he said in his commentary explaining this proof: i.e., if capability which is the condition for taklif were not before the act, but rather simultaneous, then it would necessitate the negation of sin for the disbeliever who died upon disbelief, because sin is a branch of taklif and there is no taklif (upon him) because the condition of capability is with the act (the act of faith) and there is no act as we assumed in the example. The Ash'ari response to our second proof and the refutation of it. And it was answered from the side of the Ash'aris regarding this second proof: that the condition for taklif according to us (the Ash'aris) is that the act (the locus of attachment) be capable (for the act of faith) or for its opposite (like disbelief) as in al-Mawaqif by al-`Adudi. So in the state of disbelief, even if faith is not attached to capability, disbelief which is the opposite of faith is attached to capability. Then he said in refutation (of this response): I say: The faith of the disbeliever is not impossible like the impossibility of creating atoms by the originated capability, by agreement. Indeed, the faith of the disbeliever is something the slave can manage and has power over, while creating atoms is among the impossibilities relative to the originated capability. Rather, the disbeliever according to us (the Maturidis) is like the one who is still (sakin) who has power over movement, and there is no impediment except his lack of will. And according to them (the Ash'aris), he is like the one who is tied (muqayyad) who has no power over movement, even if he wills, because the tie prevents him from movement. And since the Ash'aris said that the disbeliever in the state of disbelief is not at all capable of faith, his case is not like that of the tied one. The author turned away from his first example and brought another example and said: 'No', i.e., the disbeliever according to us is not like the stationary, and according to them like the tied, rather he is according to us like the tied, and according to them like the lame (al-azman/al-a'raj). Because the disbeliever according to us is capable of faith, but disbelief and the deep-rooted false creeds prevent him from faith, like the tied one who is capable of movement but the tie prevents him from movement. As for him according to them being like the lame, because the disbeliever is not at all capable of faith according to them, like the lame who cannot move or walk at all. And the distinction between the disbeliever and the lame—that the former is not incapable, unlike the latter—is necessary, and their denial of the difference between them and their judgment that they are equal is clear obstinacy. In summary, the statement that capability of one of the two ends of the act is the condition for taklif necessitates that
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a detailed theological-legal dispute between the Maturidi (Hanafi) school and the Ash'ari school on the nature of capability (qudra) as a condition for legal obligation (taklif). The author first presents two rational proofs from the Maturidis that capability must exist before the voluntary act. The first proof: capability is a condition for the voluntary act, and a condition is prior to its conditioned object. The second proof: if capability were simultaneous, then a disbeliever would not be obligated to faith before embracing it, because at that moment faith is not within his capability (since capability would only exist concurrently with the act of faith, which has not yet occurred). This would lead to the absurd consequence that a disbeliever who dies in disbelief would not be sinful, since sin presupposes valid obligation. The Ash'aris respond by redefining the condition: they say the condition is not that the act itself be capable, but that either the act or its opposite be capable. Thus a disbeliever, though incapable of faith, is capable of disbelief, and this suffices for obligation. The author refutes this by noting that faith is not inherently impossible like creating atoms; the disbeliever is capable of faith but chooses disbelief. He then gives an analogy: the Maturidi view is that the disbeliever is like a tied person who can move but is prevented; the Ash'ari view makes him like a lame person who inherently cannot move. The distinction is clear, and the Ash'ari position is criticized as obstinacy (mukabarah).

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): The capacity or capability of the human agent to perform an act, considered a condition for legal obligation.
التكليف (al-taklif): The imposition of a legal obligation (fard, wajib, etc.) upon a competent person.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition whose absence entails absence of the conditioned, but whose presence does not necessitate the conditioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris in this passage, what is the condition for taklif regarding capability?
A) The act itself must be capable for the obligatee.
B) The act or its opposite must be capable for the obligatee.
C) Capability must exist before the act.
D) Capability is not a condition for taklif at all.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi/Hanafi position that capability precedes the act is essential to maintain human accountability; the Ash'ari view, by making capability simultaneous, undermines the basis for sin.

The Infidel's Capacity for Faith: Maturidi vs Ash'ari
Refuting Ash'ari claim that infidel lacks capacity for faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on the infidel's capacity for faith and explain the Maturidi refutation of the Ash'ari argument using analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
The infidel has capacity for faith but is prevented by his choice; the Ash'ari view that he is like a lame person contradicts common sense.

This passage presents a key usul dispute between Maturidi and Ash'ari schools regarding whether the infidel has the capacity (qudra) to believe while in a state of unbelief. The Ash'aris argue that the condition for obligation is that the act or its opposite be within capacity, so the infidel is obligated but not capable of faith. The Maturidi refutes this by distinguishing between having capacity but being prevented, versus lacking capacity altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari argument
State the Ash'ari position: the condition for taklif is that the act be an object of capacity for either the act or its opposite. Thus, the infidel has capacity for unbelief, so he is obligated, but not for faith.
This is based on al-Mawaqif by al-‘Adudi.
2
Locate the Maturidi counter-argument
The Maturidi responds that the Ash'ari analogy is flawed. The infidel is like a stationary person who can move but does not will to, not like a bound person who cannot move even if he wills.
The key distinction is between lack of will and lack of ability.
3
Analyze the corrected analogy
The author then corrects: the infidel according to Maturidis is like a bound person (has capacity but prevented by unbelief), while according to Ash'aris he is like a lame person (no capacity at all). This shows the error.
The difference is that a bound person has intrinsic capacity; a lame person does not.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the infidel lacks capacity, he is not blameworthy for not believing; if he has capacity but chooses not to, he is blameworthy. The Maturidi view upholds moral responsibility.
This affects divine justice and punishment.
5
Reflect on the logical necessity
The distinction between incapacity and prevention is self-evident. Denying it, as the Ash'aris do according to the text, is 'clear obstinacy.'
This highlights the Maturidi commitment to rational intuition.

جواب الأشاعرة عن دليلنا الثاني والرد عليه
وأجيب من جانب الأشاعرة عن هذا الدليل الثاني بأن شرط التكليف عندنا (الأشاعرة) هو أن يكون الفعل (محل التعلق) للقدرة (على فعل الإيمان) أو على فعل ضده (كالكفر) كذا في المواقف للعضدي، ففي حال الكفر وإن لم يكن الإيمان متعلقاً للقدرة، لكن الكفر الذي هو ضد الإيمان متعلق لها للقدرة. ثم قال في الرد عليه (على هذا الجواب) أقول: إن إيمان الكافر ليست استحالته كاستحالة خلق الجواهر من القدرة الحادثة اتفاقاً، فإن إيمان الكافر مما يتمكن ويقدر عليه العبد، وخلق الجواهر من الممتنعات بالنسبة إلى القدرة الحادثة. بل الكافر عندنا (الماتريدية) كالساكن الذي يقدر على الحركة، ولا مانع عنها إلا عدم إرادته، (والكافر) عندهم (الأشاعرة) كالمقيد الذي لا يقدر على الحركة، وإن أراد؛ لأنه يمنعه القيد عن الحركة. وبما أن الأشاعرة قالوا: إن الكافر حال الكفر ليس بقادر على الإيمان أصلاً، فحاله ليس كحال المقيد، أعرض المصنف عن مثاله الأول، وجاء بمثال آخر، وقال "لا" أي ليس الكافر عندنا كالساكن، وعندهم كالمقيد، بل هو عندنا كالمقيد، وعندهم كالزمن (الأعرج) لأن الكافر عندنا قادر على الإيمان، لكن الكفر ورسوخ العقائد الباطلة، منعه عن الإيمان، كالمقيد القادر على الحركة، ولكن القيد يمنعه عن الحركة، وأما كونه عندهم كالزمن (الأعرج) فلأن الكافر ليس بقادر على الإيمان عندهم أصلاً كالزمن الذي لا يقدر على الحركة والمشي أصلاً، والتفرقة بين الكافر والزمن بأن الأول ليس بعاجز، بخلاف الثاني ضرورية، وإنكارهم الفرق بينهما والحكم بأنهما سواء مكابرة واضحة، والحاصل أن القول بمقدورية أحد طرفي الفعل هو شرط التكليف يستلزم أن يكون الإيمان غير مقدور، فيكون مثله مثل الحركة من الزمن، مع أن الفرق بديهي.
The Ash'ari response to our second proof and the refutation thereof. The Ash'aris responded to this second proof by saying that the condition of obligation according to us (the Ash'aris) is that the act be a locus of attachment for capacity (for the act of faith) or for its opposite (like unbelief), as in al-Mawaqif by al-‘Adudi. So in the state of unbelief, although faith is not an object of capacity, unbelief, which is the opposite of faith, is an object of capacity. Then he said in refutation of this response: I say: The impossibility of the infidel's faith is not like the impossibility of creating atoms from contingent power by agreement, for the infidel's faith is something the servant can have capacity for, while creating atoms is among the impossibilities for contingent power. Rather, the infidel according to us (the Maturidis) is like a stationary person who has capacity for movement and nothing prevents him except his lack of will; while the infidel according to them (the Ash'aris) is like a bound person who cannot move even if he wills because the bond prevents him from movement. And since the Ash'aris said that the infidel in the state of unbelief is not capable of faith at all, his state is not like that of a bound person; the author turned away from his first example and came with another example, saying: "No" i.e. the infidel is not like a stationary person according to us, and like a bound person according to them; rather, he is like a bound person according to us and like a lame person according to them, because the infidel according to us is capable of faith, but unbelief and the rootedness of false beliefs prevent him from faith, like a bound person who is capable of movement but the bond prevents him; and as for his being like a lame person according to them, because the infidel is not at all capable of faith according to them, like a lame person who cannot move or walk at all. And the distinction between the infidel and the lame person – that the former is not incapable while the latter is – is necessary, and their denial of the difference and judgment that they are equal is clear obstinacy. The conclusion is that the statement that capacity for one of the two sides of the act is the condition of obligation necessitates that faith be not within capacity, making it like the movement of a lame person, while the difference is self-evident.

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Maturidi (Hanafi) and Ash'ari schools on whether the infidel has capacity (qudra) for faith. The Ash'ari argument had claimed that the condition for obligation is that the act be an object of capacity for either the act or its opposite; thus, even if the infidel cannot do faith, he can do unbelief, so he is accountable. The Maturidi refutes this by distinguishing the infidel from someone physically incapable. The author uses two analogies: the stationary person (can move but doesn't) vs. the bound person (cannot move even if he wills). The Ash'aris treat the infidel as bound (no capacity for faith), but the Maturidis see him as stationary (has capacity, but unbelief prevents). The author then corrects the analogy: actually, the infidel is like a bound person (has capacity but prevented) in the Maturidi view, and like a lame person (no capacity at all) in the Ash'ari view. The key point is that the infidel's lack of faith is not due to incapacity but to choice, so he deserves blame. This shows the Maturidi position that capacity precedes the act and that the infidel is not excused.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): Capacity or power to perform an act; a condition for the validity of taklif (obligation).
المقيد (al-muqayyad): A person bound by a physical restraint, unable to move despite having intrinsic capacity.
الزمن (al-zaman): A lame or chronically disabled person who lacks the intrinsic ability to move.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Like a lame person who cannot move at all.
B) Like a bound person who has capacity but is prevented.
C) Like a stationary person who lacks all capacity.
D) Like a person who has capacity only for unbelief.

Key Takeaway
The infidel has capacity for faith but is hindered by his own unbelief; denying this capacity is contrary to reason and moral accountability.

The Ash'ari Arguments on Qudra and Hanafi Refutations
Dispute over whether capacity precedes or accompanies the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Ash'ari proofs on qudra and explain the Hanafi refutations, applying the Hanafi principle that capacity precedes the act as a potentiality.

Core Idea
Capacity (qudra) is a potentiality, not a relation to an existing act; it precedes the act and enables obligation.

This passage presents a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafi/Maturidi and Ash'ari schools on the nature of capacity (qudra) as a condition for legal obligation (taklif). The Ash'aris argue that capacity must be simultaneous with the act, while the Hanafis hold that it precedes the act as a potentiality. The author refutes three Ash'ari proofs using rational arguments and a Quranic verse. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi principle that Allah does not oblige a soul beyond its capacity, given before the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and positions
State the contested point: whether capacity (qudra) is before or with the act. Identify the Hanafi position (precedes) and the Ash'ari position (simultaneous).
This is a dispute in usul al-fiqh about the nature of a condition for taklif.
2
Examine the first Ash'ari proof and Hanafi response
The Ash'aris argue that power is a relational attribute requiring an existing object. The Hanafi response uses God's eternal power as a counterexample and clarifies that power is a potentiality.
The Quranic verse (2:286) is cited as evidence that capacity is given before obligation.
3
Examine the second Ash'ari proof and Hanafi response
The Ash'aris claim that power is an accident that does not endure. The Hanafi response denies this premise and argues that even if accidents do not endure, the universal nature of power remains through successive instances.
This response relies on the distinction between particular instances and the universal concept of power.
4
Examine the third Ash'ari proof and Hanafi response
The Ash'aris argue that an act cannot be in one's power before it exists. The Hanafi response invokes God's power over all acts before existence and holds that possibility is sufficient for human capacity.
The author declares this proof false (bāṭil) and provides counterarguments.
5
Understand al-Razi's reconciliation
Imam al-Razi distinguishes two meanings of power: mere potentiality (before the act) and effective power meeting all conditions (with the act). This resolves the dispute by showing both sides are partially correct.
This reconciliation is a useful tool for understanding the ikhtilaf.

دلائل الأشاعرة على كون القدرة مع الفعل لا قبله
وذكر المصنف لهم ثلاثة أدلة:
- قالوا أوّلاً: إنّها (القدرة ) صفة متعلقة بالمقدور كتعلق الضرب بالمضروب ووجود المتعلّق (بكسر اللام) بدون المتعلَّق بفتح اللام) محال. فكما أن تعلق الضرب و وجوده قبل وجود المضروب المفعول محال ، كذلك وجود القدرة وتعلقها بدون وجود الفعل المقدور محال، فلابد أن تكون القدرة مع الفعل الذى تتعلق به.
وأجاب المصنف عن هذا الدليل بوجهين قلنا ( أولا!) : بأنه منقوض بقدرة هي صفة البارى تعالى، فإنها كانت ثابتة وموجودة في الأزل ولم يكن شيئ من المقدور بموجود ، وإلّا ، أي وإن لم يكن كذلك لزم قدم العالم الذي هو مقدور الله تعالى، لأنه لا بد أن يكون العالم المقدور موجوداً في الأزل مع القدرة التي تتعلق به.
وأما الثاني فليست القدرة صفة متعلقة بالمقدور الموجود، بل هي (القدرة ) صفة لها صلاحية التعلّق بالمقدور ، سواء كان المقدور موجوداً أو معدوماً فلا تستدعى وجود المقدور، وقال إمام الحرمين : " من أنصف نفسه علم أن معنى القدرة، هو التمكن من الفعل ، وهذا إنما يُعقل قبل الفعل.
وقوله تعالى: (لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها يدل على أن الله تعالى أعطى كل أحدٍ سعةً وطاقة وقدرة العمل بما يريد منهم ، ثم كلفهم به.
۲ - وقالوا (الاشاعرة ( ثانياً : إنها (القدرة ) عرض ، وهو لا يبقى زمانين (حتى كانت موجودة قبل الفعل) فلو تقدمت على هذا الفعل لعدمت قبل وجوده، فلم تتعلق بالفعل عند وجوده والجواب عن هذا الدليل أيضاً بوجهين:
الأول : لانسلم عدم بقاء الأعراض إلى زمانين، فإن جمهور الفلاسفة والمعتزلة وغيرهم من العقلاء قالو ببقاء الأعراض إلى از منة طويلة ، بل قد قد مضى الزمان الذى كان يقول فيه الناس: الاعراض إذا وجدت تتلاشى وتنعدم، وكثرة وسائل حفظ الأعراض عن الفناء حفظت أعراض الناس عن عار هذا الإنكار الخطأ.
والثانى: أنه لوسلّم عدم البقاء (بقاء الامثال وجزئيات القدرة) فالشرط (أي شرط التكليف هي الطبيعة الكلية للقدرة التي تبقى بتوارد الجزئيات والأمثال فالطبيعة الكلية لا تفنى ولاتزول
٣- وقالوا ثالثاً: لا يمكن الفعل ،قبله أي قبل وجود نفسه، فلايكون الفعل مقدوراً قبل وجوده فأين تقديم القدرة على الفعل؟
وأجمل المصنف في ردّه وقال وهو باطل كماترى. فوجه بطلان هذا الدليل أنه منقوض بقدرة البارى تعالى ، فإن الأفعال كلها تحة قدرته تعالى قبل وجودها، وبعد وجودها ، فلا يتوقف تعلق قدرته بالمقدور بعد وجوده ، لتكون القدرة مع الفعل، وأيضاً وصف قبلية الفعل على نفسه ممتنع بالذات، وأما ثبوت إمكان وجود الفعل في زمان قبل زمان وجوده بالفعل، فغیر مستحيل بل ضرورى وإلا يلزم الانقلاب من الإمكان إلى الامتناع وانقلاب الحقائق من الممتنعات
فائدة
وقد جمع الإمام الرازي بين المذهبين وقال: إن القدرة يطلق على معنيين: ۱ - على مجرد القوة التى هي مبدأ الأفعال المختلفة، ولا شك أن نسبتها إلى الضدين ( كالكفر والإيمان) سواء، وهي قبل الفعل.
۲- على القوة المستجمعة شرائط التأثير، ولو عادةً ، ولاشك أنها لا تتعلق بالضدين معاً، بل هي بالنسبة إلى كلّ مقدور غيرها بالنسبة إلى المقدور الآخر، وإنها مع الفعل، فلعل الشيخ الأشعرى أراد بالقدرة القدرة المستجمعة ، والمعتزلة (أرادوا) مجرد القدرة التي تكون مع الفعل، انتهى كلام الرازي
: المسألة المتفرعة على كون القدرة قبل الفعل أو معه القدرة يتعلق بالأمور المتضادة، خلافاً لهم مطلقاً، لا معاً ولا بدلاً
Faith is not within power, so it is like movement from time, although the difference is obvious. The proofs of the Ash'aris that power is with the act, not before it: The author mentioned for them three proofs. First: They said that power is a quality related to the empowered object, like the relation of striking to the struck object, and existence of the related (with kasr of the lam) without the related (with fath of the lam) is impossible. So just as the relation of striking and its existence before the existence of the struck object is impossible, so too the existence of power and its relation without the existence of the act is impossible; thus power must be with the act to which it relates. The author answered this proof in two ways, we said first: that it is refuted by the power which is an attribute of Allah the Exalted, for it was established and existed in pre-eternity while none of the empowered existed. Otherwise, if not so, the eternity of the world would follow, which is the empowered of Allah, because the world must exist in pre-eternity with the power that relates to it. Second: Power is not a quality related to an existing empowered; rather, it is a quality with the potential to relate to the empowered, whether the empowered exists or not, so it does not require the existence of the empowered. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Whoever is fair to himself knows that the meaning of power is capability to act, and this is only conceivable before the act.' And His statement (do not burden a soul beyond its capacity) indicates that Allah has given everyone capacity, energy, and power to do what He wants from them, then He obligated them. Second: They said (the Ash'aris) that power is an accident, and it does not last for two moments (so it was not present before the act). If it preceded the act, it would cease before its existence, so it would not relate to the act when it exists. The answer to this proof is also in two ways: First: we do not concede that accidents do not last for two moments; for the majority of philosophers and Mu'tazilites and other rational people said that accidents last for a long time, indeed there was a time when people said that accidents when they occur disintegrate and cease, and the abundance of means of preserving accidents from perdition preserved people's honor from the shame of this erroneous denial. Second: if we concede non-endurance (but the endurance of similars and particulars of power), the condition (i.e., condition of obligation) is the universal nature of power, which endures through the succession of particulars and similars; the universal nature does not perish nor cease. Third: They said: the act cannot be before it, i.e., before its own existence, so the act is not in one's power before its existence; so where is the precedence of power over the act? The author summarized his response saying: it is false as you see. The face of its falsity is that it is refuted by the power of Allah the Exalted, for all acts are under His power before their existence and after their existence; thus His power relating to the empowered does not wait for its existence so that power would be with the act. Also, the description of an act being before itself is impossible in itself. As for establishing the possibility of the act's existence at a time before its actual existence, it is not impossible but necessary; otherwise the change from possibility to impossibility would follow, and changing realities is among the impossible. Benefit: Imam al-Razi reconciled the two schools and said: power is used for two meanings: 1) mere strength which is the principle of various acts, and there is no doubt that its relation to opposites (like disbelief and faith) is equal, and it is before the act. 2) strength that meets the conditions of effectiveness, even customarily, and there is no doubt that it does not relate to both opposites simultaneously, but with respect to each empowered it is different from its respect to another empowered, and it is with the act. Perhaps al-Ash'ari meant by power the comprehensive power, and the Mu'tazilites meant mere power that is with the act. End of al-Razi's speech. The subsidiary issue on whether power is before or with the act: power relates to contradictory matters, contrary to them absolutely, not collectively nor alternatively.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents a classic theological-legal dispute within usul al-fiqh regarding the nature of capacity (qudra) as a condition for taklif (obligation). The Ash'ari school argues that power must be contemporaneous with the act, not prior to it. The author of the commentary, responding to the matn, presents three Ash'ari proofs and refutes each from a Hanafi-Maturidi perspective. First Proof: Power is a relational attribute that requires its object; just as striking requires a struck object, power requires an existing act. The Hanafi refutation points out that this is refuted by God's eternal power, which existed before any creation. Moreover, power is a capacity or potentiality that can exist without its object; it is not a relation to an existing thing. The verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is cited as evidence that capacity is given before the obligation. Second Proof: Power is an accident that does not endure; if it preceded the act, it would perish before the act occurs. The Hanafi response denies that accidents must cease quickly; many philosophers and the Mu'tazila hold that accidents can endure. Even if we concede non-endurance, the condition for obligation is the universal nature of power, which continues through successive instances. Third Proof: An act cannot be described as being within power before it exists because it is not yet there. The refutation invokes God's power, which encompasses acts before they exist. For human acts, it is not necessary that the act itself exist prior, but rather the possibility of its existence at a future time is sufficient; otherwise, impossibility would become possibility, which is absurd. The passage concludes with a reconciliation by Imam al-Razi, distinguishing two meanings of power: mere potentiality (before the act) and effective power with all conditions (with the act). This resolves the dispute by showing that both sides are partially correct but speaking of different meanings. The subsidiary issue is that power can relate to contradictory matters (like faith and disbelief) in terms of potentiality, but not both simultaneously in terms of effective power.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): The capacity or power to perform an act, considered as a condition for taklif (obligation).
التكليف (al-taklīf): The imposition of a legal obligation by Allah upon a legally responsible person.
العرض (al-‘araḍ): An accident; a non-substantial attribute that inheres in a substance and is usually considered impermanent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this text, when does the capacity (qudra) for an act exist relative to the act itself?
A) Simultaneously with the act
B) Before the act, as a potentiality
C) Only after the act is completed
D) At the moment of obligation, not before

Key Takeaway
Capacity (qudra) is a potentiality that precedes the act; this is supported by rational arguments and Quran 2:286.

The Single Power and Opposite Acts: Maturidi vs Ash'ari
Exposition of usul dispute with al-Razi's reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on whether a single human power can relate to opposite acts, and apply al-Razi's two-sense distinction of power to clarify the dispute.

Core Idea
A single power can relate to opposites before the act; it becomes specific only when conditions of effect are gathered.

In the study of usul al-fiqh, the nature of human capacity (qudra) is crucial for understanding moral responsibility and the validity of acts. One key debate is whether a single power can be the source of opposite actions, such as faith versus disbelief or standing versus sitting. This passage presents the Maturidi (Hanafi) view, the Ash'ari counterview, and Imam al-Razi's reconciliatory distinction between two meanings of power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute is about whether a single human power (qudra wāḥida) can relate to opposite actions (al-umūr al-mutadāda), either simultaneously or consecutively.
Opposites include pairs like faith/disbelief, truth/falsehood, movement/stillness.
2
Extract the Maturidi–Mu'tazili position
Find the passage that states this school affirms that a single power can relate to opposites. Note their rational basis: power before the act is an indeterminate strength indifferent to opposites.
Al-Razi's first sense of power is 'mere strength' (mujarrad al-quwwa).
3
Extract the Ash'ari position
Find the Ash'ari denial: they hold that each act requires a specific power created concurrently with the act, so a single power cannot relate to opposites together or alternately.
Al-Razi's second sense is 'strength with conditions of effect' (al-quwwa al-mustajmi'a sharāʾiṭ al-ta'thīr).
4
Understand al-Razi's reconciliation
Analyze the two definitions of power: (1) universal capacity before the act, which is neutral to opposites; (2) specific capability with the act, which is unique to each opposite. Recognize that each school is focusing on a different sense.
This reconciliation shows that the dispute is partly terminological.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Apply the difference to a fiqh scenario: if a person begins a prayer with one intention (e.g., fard) and then changes to another (e.g., nafl), the Maturidi view allows the same power to be repurposed, while the Ash'ari view would require a new power for the new act.
This affects rulings on continuity and validity of changing intentions within an act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان يقول فيه الناس: الاعراض إذا وجدت تتلاشى وتنعدم، وكثرة وسائل حفظ الأعراض عن الفناء حفظت أعراض الناس عن عار هذا الإنكار الخطأ. والثانى: أنه لوسلّم عدم البقاء (بقاء الامثال وجزئيات القدرة) فالشرط (أي شرط التكليف هي الطبيعة الكلية للقدرة التي تبقى بتوارد الجزئيات والأمثال فالطبيعة الكلية لا تفنى ولاتزول
٣- وقالوا ثالثاً: لا يمكن الفعل ،قبله أي قبل وجود نفسه، فلايكون الفعل مقدوراً قبل وجوده فأين تقديم القدرة على الفعل؟
وأجمل المصنف في ردّه وقال وهو باطل كماترى. فوجه بطلان هذا الدليل أنه منقوض بقدرة البارى تعالى ، فإن الأفعال كلها تحة قدرته تعالى قبل وجودها، وبعد وجودها ، فلا يتوقف تعلق قدرته بالمقدور بعد وجوده ، لتكون القدرة مع الفعل، وأيضاً وصف قبلية الفعل على نفسه ممتنع بالذات، وأما ثبوت إمكان وجود الفعل في زمان قبل زمان وجوده بالفعل، فغیر مستحيل بل ضرورى وإلا يلزم الانقلاب من الإمكان إلى الامتناع وانقلاب الحقائق من الممتنعات

وقد جمع الإمام الرازي بين المذهبين وقال: إن القدرة يطلق على معنيين: ۱ - على مجرد القوة التى هي مبدأ الأفعال المختلفة، ولا شك أن نسبتها إلى الضدين ( كالكفر والإيمان) سواء، وهي قبل الفعل.
۲- على القوة المستجمعة شرائط التأثير، ولو عادةً ، ولاشك أنها لا تتعلق بالضدين معاً، بل هي بالنسبة إلى كلّ مقدور غيرها بالنسبة إلى المقدور الآخر، وإنها مع الفعل، فلعل الشيخ الأشعرى أراد بالقدرة القدرة المستجمعة ، والمعتزلة (أرادوا) مجرد القدرة التي تكون مع الفعل، انتهى كلام الرازي
: المسألة المتفرعة على كون القدرة قبل الفعل أو معه القدرة يتعلق بالأمور المتضادة، خلافاً لهم مطلقاً، لا معاً ولا بدلاً
القدرة (الواحدة ( تتعلق بالأمور المتضادة ) كالكفر والإيمان والصدق والكذب، والحركة والسكون والقيام والقعود) (۱) وهذا عند الماتريدية والمعتزلة (۲)، خلافاً لهم أي للأشاعرة، فإنّهم لا يقولون بتعلق القدرة الواحدة بالأمور المتضادة مطلقاً لامعاً ، بأن يكون نسبتها إلى الضدين سوائ، ولا بدلاً بأن يتعلق اوّلاً بضد وثانياً بضد آخر، بل كل قدرة مختصة بضد لا يوجد ذلك الضد الا معها.
He used to say about it: the accidents if they occur, they disintegrate and cease, and the abundance of means of preserving accidents from annihilation preserved the people's honor from the disgrace of this mistaken denial. The second: if non-permanence is conceded (the permanence of similars and participles of power), then the condition (i.e., the condition of obligation is the universal nature of power which remains by the succession of participles and similars, so the universal nature does not perish nor cease. 3- They said thirdly: the act cannot be before it, i.e., before its own existence, so the act is not within power before its existence, so where is the precedence of power over the act? The author summarized his refutation and said: it is false as you see. The face of the invalidity of this proof is that it is refuted by the power of the Exalted Creator, for all acts are under His power before their existence and after their existence, so His power's attachment to the object capable after its existence is not conditioned, so that power is with the act. Also, describing the act's priority over itself is intrinsically impossible. As for establishing the possibility of the act's existence in a time before its actual time of existence, that is not impossible but necessary; otherwise, a change from possibility to impossibility would follow, which is among the impossibilities. Benefit: Imam al-Razi reconciled the two schools and said: power is used in two senses: 1- on mere strength which is the starting point of different actions, and there is no doubt that its relation to the two opposites (like disbelief and faith) is equal, and it is before the act. 2- on the strength that has gathered the conditions of effect, even customarily, and no doubt that it does not relate to both opposites together, but its relation to each object capable is other than its relation to the other object, and it is with the act. So perhaps al-Ash'ari intended by power the gathered strength, and the Mu'tazila intended the mere strength which is with the act. End of al-Razi's words. The branch issue: based on whether power is before or with the act, [it is that] power relates to opposite matters, contrary to them absolutely, neither together nor alternately. The single power relates to opposite matters (like disbelief and faith, truthfulness and falsehood, movement and stillness, standing and sitting) (1). This is with the Maturidis and the Mu'tazila (2), contrary to them, i.e., the Ash'aris, for they do not hold the attachment of a single power to opposite matters absolutely, neither together, meaning that its relation to the two opposites is equal, nor alternately, meaning that it attaches first to one opposite and second to another opposite; rather, each power is specific to an opposite, that opposite does not exist except with it.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a key usul dispute regarding the nature of human capacity (qudra) and its relation to actions, particularly opposite actions. The author begins by summarizing three objections against the view that power precedes the act, then refutes them using rational arguments, including an analogy with God's power and the impossibility of the act preceding itself. The refutation is followed by a quote from Imam al-Razi who reconciles the two positions (Ash'ari and Mu'tazila) by distinguishing two meanings of power: (1) mere strength that is the source of diverse actions, which exists before the act and can relate to opposites equally, and (2) strength that has gathered the conditions of effective action, which is specific to each particular act and exists with it. This sets the stage for the derivative issue (mas'ala mutafarri'a) on whether a single power can relate to opposite matters. The author states the Maturidi and Mu'tazili position that a single power can relate to opposites, and contrasts it with the Ash'ari position that denies this absolutely—whether simultaneously or consecutively. For Ash'aris, each power is uniquely tied to its specific opposite and cannot be transferred to another. This dispute has practical implications for how human agency and moral responsibility are understood in relation to acts like faith, disbelief, truth, and falsehood.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra): Human capacity or power to act. In this passage, two senses: (1) mere strength as a source of actions, indifferent to opposites; (2) strength with conditions of effect, specific to one action.
الأمور المتضادة (al-umūr al-mutadāda): Opposing actions or qualities that cannot coexist, such as faith and disbelief, truth and falsehood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A single power can relate to two opposites simultaneously.
B) A single power before the act can be the source of either opposite.
C) Each opposite requires a separate power created at the moment.
D) Power does not exist before the act.

Key Takeaway
The key to the dispute is that 'power' can mean either universal capacity (before act) or specific capability (with act).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Single Capacity for Opposites: A Theological Dispute
States the dispute on whether a single capacity relates to contradictory actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of the Maturidis/Mu'tazila and Ash'aris on whether a single capacity can relate to contradictory actions, and explain the relevance of this theological dispute to Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A single capacity can relate to opposites: the Maturidi view, adopted by Hanafis, versus Ash'ari specificity.

This lesson unpacks a foundational theological disagreement between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools regarding human capacity (qudra). While this is a kalam (theology) issue, it directly influences how Hanafi usul al-fiqh understands the conditions of taklif (legal obligation). The passage states the two positions concisely, without argumentation, leaving us to appreciate the difference and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being disputed? The passage asks: can a single capacity (one power) be directed to two opposite actions (e.g., belief vs. disbelief, sitting vs. standing)?
The dispute is about the nature of human agency, not about specific legal rules.
2
State the Maturidi/Mu'tazila position
These schools affirm that the same capacity can relate to opposites both simultaneously (neutral ability to choose either) and alternately (first one, then the other).
Hanafis typically follow the Maturidi school in theology.
3
State the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris reject both modes: a capacity is specific to a single opposite and cannot be used for the other, even sequentially.
This leads to the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) where human power is created at the moment of action.
4
Recognize the absence of proofs
The passage merely states the positions without citing evidence. The Maturidi proof is rational: if capacity were specific, a person could never choose between actions, negating moral responsibility. The Ash'ari proof relies on the contingency of human power upon divine creation.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) dispute.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul
Hanafi usul al-fiqh, influenced by Maturidi theology, assumes that a single capacity can relate to opposites. This underpins the view that a person can be obligated to perform contradictory commands (e.g., pray and not steal) because the capacity is not exhausted by one act.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical result) affects the theory of taklif and the definition of istita'a (ability).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة الواحدة تتعلق بالأمور المتضادة كالكفر والإيمان والصدق والكذب، والحركة والسكون والقيام والقعود وهذا عند الماتريدية والمعتزلة، خلافاً لهم أي للأشاعرة، فإنّهم لا يقولون بتعلق القدرة الواحدة بالأمور المتضادة مطلقاً لا معاً، بأن يكون نسبتها إلى الضدين سواء، ولا بدلاً بأن يتعلق أوّلاً بضد وثانياً بضد آخر، بل كل قدرة مختصة بضد لا يوجد ذلك الضد إلا معها.
The one capacity relates to contradictory things like disbelief and faith, truth and falsehood, movement and stillness, standing and sitting. This is according to the Maturidis and Mu'tazila, contrary to them, i.e., the Ash'aris, for they do not say that a single capacity relates to contradictory things absolutely, neither simultaneously, meaning its relation to the two opposites is equal, nor alternately, meaning it relates first to one opposite and then to another opposite, rather every capacity is specific to one opposite; that opposite does not exist except with it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute regarding the capacity (qudra) of the servant. The Maturidi and Mu'tazili schools hold that a single capacity can relate to two opposite actions, such as belief and disbelief, or sitting and standing. The Ash'aris disagree entirely: they assert that each capacity is specifically tied to one opposite and cannot relate to the other, whether simultaneously or consecutively. This is a matter of kalam (theology) that has implications for the nature of human action and divine predestination. The author clearly states the positions without preference here.

Key Terms
القدرة الواحدة (al-qudra al-wāḥida): The single capacity of a human being to perform an action, considered as one power.
الأمور المتضادة (al-umūr al-mutaḍādda): Contradictory things, i.e., two actions or states that cannot coexist, like belief and disbelief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, each capacity is specific to one opposite only.
C) Only if the person is under compulsion.
D) Only if the actions are similar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school adopts the Maturidi view that a single capacity can relate to opposites, which has implications for usul al-fiqh, particularly the validity of taklif with multiple commands.

The Obligation of Qada' for One Who Becomes Mukallaf in the Final Part of Time
Dispute and resolution on qada' for a late mukallaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument for qada' being wajib for a late mukallaf and distinguish the verified view from dialectical responses.

Core Idea
Qada' is wajib not because performance was possible, but because the obligation itself was established at the last part.

This lesson examines a nuanced usul dispute: Is qada' obligatory for someone who becomes a mukallaf (accountable) in the final, indivisible part of the prayer time? Zufar argues no, because performance is impossible. The Hanafi majority says yes. We will dissect the arguments, including Ibn al-Humam's appeal to possible extension and the author's critique, and finally arrive at the verified position: qada' follows from the original obligation (wujūb), not from a part of the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
What is the exact scenario? The individuals are a disbeliever who converts, a child who reaches puberty, or an insane person who regains sanity at the very end of a prayer time. The dispute is about whether qada' is wajib for the prayer whose time has just expired.
Zufar's opposition is within the Hanafi school.
2
State Zufar's position and proof
Zufar argues that since the last part cannot contain the act, performance is impossible, so the act was never wajib to perform. Therefore, qada' is not wajib.
This is based on the principle that qada' is only for acts that were wajib to perform.
3
Examine Ibn al-Humam's rebuttal and the author's critique
Ibn al-Humam says qada' is wajib because we are not certain the part is truly last; time could extend. The author (Badakhshani) critiques: (1) the miracle of Joshua is not analogous; (2) extension either increases parts (possible) or stretches the indivisible part (impossible); (3) the real last part (waqi'i) is known, not the hypothetical one.
The author shows that Ibn al-Humam's argument is dialectical, not verificatory.
4
Understand the dialectical response to the objection of fit
Even if the last part seems too short for a prolonged act, the author offers a philosophical argument (fast and slow motion occur in the same time) to silence the opponent. He admits this is only for argument, not the true position.
Do not mistake this as the verified view.
5
Grasp the verified view (al-haqq)
The true position: Qada' is wajib based on the original obligation (wujūb) itself, just like a sleeping person who misses prayer. Alternatively, some argue based on obligation of a part, but the author hints that is weak. The strongest basis is the wujūb that attached at the last part.
This is the mufta bihi position among Hanafis.
