القدم في الكلام المشترك. وقوله: فتدبر لعله إشارة إلى أن التقسيم بالنسبة إلى العوارض على نحوين: تقسيم باعتبار رعاية العوارض قبل عروضها، وتقسيم بعد عروض العوارض، والفرق أن الأول تقسيم من ابتداء الأمر، والثاني تقسيم بعد عروض العوارض. وأيضا (يلزم عليه أن لا يكون المعدوم حينئذ مكلفا، إذ لا تعلق للكلام الأزلي بأمر أو نهي وغيرهما في الأزل. وقالوا ثانيا: إن تكليف المعدوم فرع قدم الكلام الإلهي بأقسامه من الأمر والنهي وغيرهما، وقدم الكلام بأقسامه محال؛ لأنه يلزم قدم عدم التناهي، يعني يلزم منه كون الأمور غير المتناهية قديمة، إذ أقسام الكلام باعتبار المتعلق (من أنواع الكلام من الاستفهام والنداء والأمر والنهي) فإن الكلام (الأمر) المتعلق بزيد غير الكلام المتعلق بعمرو، ضرورة تغاير الإضافة باعتبار تغاير المضاف إليه، وهو متعلقها (متعلق الإضافة). ثم ذكر المصنف الجواب عن دليلهم وقال: لا تعدد في الكلام بالذات، وإنما التعدد فيه بحسب تعدد المتعلقات، وهو تعدد اعتباري، فإن الكلام (كلام الله) صفة واحدة أزلية كعلمه وقدرته، وانقسام الكلام إلى الأنواع والأفراد إنما يكون باعتبار المتعلقات، لا بحسب الاختلاف ذاتا، بأن يكون كل نوع أو فرد ذاتا واحدة، خذ هذا. والتعلقات اعتبارية فالتعدد بحسبها أيضا اعتباري وقدم الأمور الاعتبارية لا بأس به، فإنها تنتهي بانتهاء الاعتبار، والمراد بالأنواع الأمر والنهي والاستفهام وغيرهما، وبالأفراد أمر تعلق بزيد، ونهي تعلق بعمرو.
By considering the attachment of those divisions to it, and it is permissible for that common thing to be free from attachment to those divisions, as it was in pre-existence before the occurrence of command, prohibition, etc., due to the temporality of that attachment. Then the author raised an objection to this inference, and to its refutation in another manner, and said: I say in refutation of this answer in another way: we grant that those divisions are not types of the ancient speech such that its existence is impossible without them, but there is no doubt that they are divisions of the common ancient speech, and it is the divider of them, but there is no doubt that the existence of the divided (the common ancient speech) without some division is impossible. This is if the division is before consideration of attributes. But if the division is by consideration of attributes, then it follows — against Abdullah bin Sa'id — the statement of the existence of some division without these attributes, which is inconceivable, because he said the existence of the divided without these attributes, so the existence of some division without these attributes also follows, because 'some division' is an expression of something with an attribute from attributes, so the existence of some division is the existence of some attribute. So the statement of the existence of some division without these attributes is a statement of the existence of some attribute without some attribute — this is a contradiction, for the existence of a thing along with its non-existence is impossible. While he (Abdullah bin Sa'id) said that the ancient is the common speech, without any division — this is a circular argument that is fit to be cast aside and not to be considered, because he assumed pre-eternity in common speech. And his saying 'ponder' perhaps indicates that the division with respect to attributes is of two ways: a division considering the attributes before their occurrence, and a division after the occurrence of attributes, and the difference is that the first is a division from the beginning, and the second is a division after the occurrence of attributes. Also (it follows for him that the nonexistent would not then be obligated, because there is no attachment of the eternal speech to a command, prohibition, etc., in pre-existence. And they said, secondly: that the obligation of the nonexistent is subsidiary to the pre-eternity of divine speech with its divisions of command, prohibition, etc., and the pre-eternity of speech with its divisions is impossible, because it necessitates pre-eternity of non-ending, meaning it necessitates that infinite matters are eternal, since the divisions of speech by consideration of the relation (from the types of speech: interrogation, address, command, prohibition) — for the speech (command) related to Zayd is other than the speech related to Amr, necessarily because of the difference of relation by the difference of the related term, which is its relation. Then the author mentioned the answer to their proof and said: There is no multiplicity in speech in essence; rather the multiplicity in it is according to the multiplicity of the relations, and it is a consideration-based multiplicity. For speech (the speech of Allah) is one eternal attribute like His knowledge and power, and the division of speech into types and individuals is only by consideration of the relations, not by difference in essence, such that each type or individual is an essence by itself. Take this. And the relations are consideration-based, so the multiplicity according to them is also consideration-based, and the pre-eternity of consideration-based matters is not problematic, for they end with the end of consideration. And by 'types' are meant command, prohibition, interrogation, etc., and by 'individuals' is a command related to Zayd and a prohibition related to Amr.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute regarding the nature of divine speech (kalam Allah) and its divisions. The subject is whether the divisions—such as command, prohibition, interrogation—are essential properties (dhātī) of the eternal speech or merely attributed aspects (iʿtibārī). The author presents and refutes the argument of Abdullah bin Sa'id, who holds that the ancient speech is common to all divisions but cannot exist without a division. The opposing argument is that if divisions are not essential but by consideration, then the common speech could exist without a division, which is absurd. The Hanafi response, given by the author, affirms that the divine speech is one eternal attribute (like knowledge and power), and multiplicity arises only from the multiplicity of its relations (mutaʿalliqāt). These relations are mental considerations (iʿtibārāt), so the multiplicity is also mental. Therefore, it is valid to speak of the eternal speech as common to all divisions without entailing essential multiplicity. The passage also links this to the issue of taklīf al-maʿdūm (obligation of the nonexistent), showing that the Muʿtazilī position leads to problems. The Hanafi position preserves divine simplicity and avoids infinite regress.

Key Terms
كلام قديم (kalām qadīm): The eternal divine speech, an attribute of Allah, one in essence.
متعلقات (mutaʿalliqāt): The relations or attachments of divine speech to different objects (e.g., command to Zayd).
اعتبارى (iʿtibārī): Mental consideration; not real existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the nature of the multiplicity in divine speech?
A) Essential multiplicity in the divine essence
B) Multiplicity by attribution to relations
C) Multiplicity in the types but not individuals
D) Multiplicity only in the form of letters and sounds

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one attribute; its division into commands and prohibitions is by consideration of relations, not essential.

Validity of Obligation When Condition of Occurrence is Absent
Usul dispute on taklif when condition of occurrence is absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on taklif when a condition for occurrence is missing and evaluate the rational proof (dalil aqli) used to support it.

Core Idea
An obligation is valid as long as the act is intrinsically possible (imkan dhati), even if its occurrence is prevented by an external factor.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: can a legal obligation be valid if the Lawgiver knows that a condition for the act's external occurrence will be absent? The Hanafi school, along with the majority of scholars, affirms validity, grounding their position in the distinction between intrinsic possibility and external impossibility. You will learn the rational proof (qiyas) they employ and how they respond to the objections of the Mu'tazila and Imam al-Haramayn.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the dispute
Identify that the Hanafi/majority hold obligation valid even when the condition for occurrence is absent, as long as the act is intrinsically possible (imkan dhati).
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
The Mu'tazila and Imam al-Haramayn argue that what lacks its condition is impossible, and impossibility precludes valid obligation.
3
Follow the Hanafi rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is a logical entailment: if it were invalid, no one would know they are obligated before the time, contradicting consensus on the obligation to begin with intention.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the objection from knowledge
They respond that knowledge does not affect possibility; the commanded's knowledge is different because it removes the benefit (motivation), not because of impossibility.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference affects the validity of pre-performance obligations: Hanafi considers it valid, opponents deem it invalid.

مسألة التكليف بالفعل الممكن الذي انتفى شرط وقوعه عند الأمر خلافاً للجمهور؟ الفعل الممكن الذي تمت شرائط وجوبه إذا علم الآمر انتفاء شرط وقوعه عند وقته هل يصح التكليف به؟ قال الجمهور يصح للمعتزلة، Issue: Obligation of a possible act whose condition for occurrence is absent at the time of command. [The] possible act which has completed the conditions of its obligation, if the commander knows that the condition for its occurrence is absent at its time, is it valid to obligate it? The Majority said: it is valid, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the Imam. And in case of ignorance, it is valid by agreement. It cannot be said: It has preceded that consensus is established on the validity of obligating what God knows will not occur. It is known that everything that does not occur is due to the absence of a condition from an eternal will or a temporal will. So the recounting of the dispute is contradictory. For we say: That consensus is with regard to intrinsic possibility, as indicated by the speech of some verifiers when transmitting consensus, where he said: Even if some people think it is impossible due to another, the dispute here is about occurrence after agreement on validity, [meaning] intrinsic validity. For us: If it were not valid, no one would know that he is obligated before the time of the act due to the possibility that a condition may not exist. Some people have denied knowledge of obligation before it, but that is false because of consensus on the realization of obligation before ability, evidenced by the obligation to begin with the intention of performing an obligatory act by consensus, which is a branch of the realization of obligation. They said first: That whose condition is absent is impossible, and possibility is a condition of obligation. We said: The condition is customary possibility, which does not preclude impossibility due to another. Also it is refuted by the commander's ignorance of the absence of the condition in reality, for knowledge has no bearing on possibility and impossibility; it follows the known. And secondly: If it were valid with the commander's knowledge, it would be valid with the knowledge of the commanded, because the failure to happen is common, and the necessary consequence is false by agreement. We said: Rather, due to absence of benefit. The possible act which has completed the conditions of its existence when the commander (the Lawgiver) knows that the condition for its occurrence is absent at its time, is it valid to obligate it or not? The Majority said: it is valid, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Imam al-Haramayn. The dispute is only concerning the condition of occurrence which was not a condition for the obligation of the act. For when the condition of its obligation is absent, or when the commander knows that the condition of obligation is absent, obligation is not realized, since obligation cannot be conceived without obligation necessarily. And in the case where the commander is ignorant of the absence of the condition of the act's occurrence, obligation is valid by agreement. [Example:] Like someone who commanded his slave to sew a garment tomorrow while he is ignorant of the survival of the slave's life until tomorrow. When Ibn al-Humam objected in al-Tahrir and said: It has preceded (in chapter three in the issue of impossibility of obligating the impossible) that consensus is established on the validity of obligating what God knows will not occur from the obligated (like the oaths of Abu Jahl). And it is known that everything that does not occur from the obligated is either its non-occurrence due to absence of a condition of its occurrence from an eternal will (which is God's will, i.e. God did not will its occurrence) or from a temporal will (which is the slave's will, i.e. the slave did not will it). So recounting the dispute between the Majority and the Mu'tazila regarding obligating that whose condition of occurrence is absent at its time is contradictory and nullifies this consensus. The author replied and said: It cannot be said (thus): For we say in response that that consensus (on the validity of obligating what God knows will not occur) was with regard to intrinsic possibility, i.e. that obligation is intrinsically possible (as indicated by) (that what is meant by validity is intrinsic possibility) the speech of some verifiers when transmitting consensus on the validity of this obligation, where
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute concerning the validity of a legal obligation (taklif) to perform an act when the commander (the Lawgiver) knows that a condition for the act's actual occurrence will be absent at the appointed time. The central question is whether such an obligation is valid in the Hanafi view and among the majority, or invalid as held by the Mu'tazila and Imam al-Haramayn. The author explains that the dispute is limited to cases where the missing condition is a condition for the act's occurrence, not for its obligation. If the condition for obligation itself is missing, there is no obligation at all. The Hanafi position is that obligation is valid as long as the act is intrinsically possible (imkan dhati), even if its occurrence is prevented by an external factor (imtina' bi-ghayrihi). The proof is that if it were not valid, people would never know they are obligated before the time of the act, since they cannot be certain conditions will exist. This would undermine the very concept of obligation before the time of performance, which is established by consensus (ijma') on the obligation to begin the act with the intention of performing a duty. The opponents argue that what has no condition is impossible, and possibility is a condition of obligation. The Hanafi response is that the required possibility is customary possibility (imkan 'adi), which is not negated by impossibility due to an external factor. Moreover, the argument is refuted by the case where the commander is ignorant of the missing condition, in which obligation is valid by agreement. The text also addresses an objection by Ibn al-Humam, who claimed that recounting this dispute contradicts the established consensus that obligation is valid even when God knows the act will not occur (e.g., the oath of Abu Jahl). The author responds that the consensus is based on intrinsic possibility, not on actual occurrence. Thus, the dispute here is about a different aspect: whether the act remains valid when a specific condition for its external occurrence is absent. The Hanafi school, following the majority, affirms validity in such cases, emphasizing that the obligation pertains to the act's intrinsic possibility and the command's intent, not to certainty of actual performance.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklif): The imposition of a legal obligation upon a mukallaf (legally responsible person).
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkan al-dhati): Intrinsic possibility: the act is possible in itself, irrespective of external factors.
شرط الوقوع (shart al-wuqu'): A condition for the actual occurrence of the act, as opposed to a condition for its obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, what is the basis for the validity of obligating an act whose condition for occurrence is known to be absent?
A) The act is intrinsically possible (imkan dhati).
B) The commander is ignorant of the missing condition.
C) The condition for occurrence is also a condition for obligation.
D) The commanded person's knowledge of the missing condition.

Key Takeaway
An obligation is valid at the time of command if the act is intrinsically possible, even if external conditions for its occurrence are absent; the key distinction is between intrinsic possibility and actual occurrence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Validity of Command When Condition of Occurrence is Absent
Usul dispute on taklif with absent condition of occurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential possibility (imkan dhati) and customary possibility (imkan 'adi) and apply this distinction to evaluate the validity of a command when the Lawgiver knows the condition of occurrence will be absent.

Core Idea
A command is valid if the act is essentially possible, even if its occurrence condition is absent.

This lesson examines a classical usul dispute: can Allah command an act that He knows will not actually occur because a condition of its occurrence is absent? The Hanafi and Ash'ari majority affirm this, while the Mu'tazila deny it. The key to resolving the dispute lies in the distinction between two types of possibility: essential (imkan dhati) and customary (imkan 'adi). By the end, you will understand why the majority holds the command valid and how this consensus is reconciled with apparent objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
The dispute concerns a command (taklif) for an act whose condition of occurrence (shart al-wuqoo') is known by the Lawgiver to be absent at the appointed time. Note that the condition of obligation (shart al-wujub) is not missing; only the condition of actual occurrence is absent.
This is not about commanding the impossible (muhal), but about an essentially possible act that will not happen due to external factors.
2
Understand the Two Types of Possibility
Essential possibility: the act is not inherently contradictory (e.g., a square circle is impossible; sewing a garment is essentially possible). Customary possibility: the act can normally occur given typical conditions. The majority holds that only essential possibility is required for the validity of the command.
The Mu'tazila require customary possibility, i.e., that the condition of occurrence be present.
3
Examine the Majority's Evidence
The majority cites ijma: commanding what Allah knows will not occur (e.g., the oaths of Abu Jahl) is valid by consensus. They also argue from the obligation to begin intention (niyya) before ability in prayer and fasting, showing knowledge of obligation precedes performance.
The ijma dalil is qat'i in thubut and dalala, supporting the majority position.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazila's Objection and the Response
The Mu'tazila argue that absence of a condition makes the act impossible, so possibility (a condition of taklif) is absent. The Hanafi response: the relevant condition for taklif is customary possibility, not essential. Customary possibility does not require the condition of occurrence to be present; the act is still possible in the customary sense if normally it would occur under typical conditions.
The response also notes that the commander's knowledge does not affect the possibility of the act itself; possibility follows the known, not the knowledge.
5
Resolve the Apparent Contradiction with Consensus
Ibn al-Humam objected that the dispute contradicts the consensus on commanding what Allah knows will not occur. The author reconciles: the consensus refers to essential possibility (the command is valid in essence), while the dispute is about actual occurrence (wuqoo' 'amalan). All agree on essential possibility; the disagreement is over whether such a command results in a real obligation in practice.
Thus, the dispute is about the thamarah (practical consequence), not the asl (principle of possibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا أولاً: ما عدم شرطه (فهو) غير ممكن، والإمكان شرط التكليف. قلنا: الشرط (هو) الإمكان العادي، وهو لا ينافي الامتناع لغيره، وأيضا منقوض بجهل الآمر بعدم الشرط في الواقع، إذ لا دخل للعلم في الإمكان والامتناع، فإنه تابع للمعلوم. وثانياً: لو صح مع علم الآمر لصح مع علم المأمور؛ لأن عدم الحصول مشترك، واللازم باطل اتفاقاً. قلنا: بل لانتفاء الفائدة.

الفعل الممكن الذي تمت شرائط لزوم وجوده إذا علم الأمر (الشارع) انتفاء (عدم) وجود شرط وقوعه عند وقته، هل يصح التكليف به أم لا؟ قال الجمهور: يصح خلافاً للمعتزلة وإمام الحرمين، وإنما يكون النزاع في شرط الوقوع الذي لم يكن شرطاً لوجوب الفعل، إذ عند انتفاء شرط وجوبه أو عند علم الأمر بانتفاء شرط الوجوب لا يتحقق التكليف، إذ لا يتصور التكليف بدون الوجوب ضرورة الوجود. وفي صورة عدم علم الأمر بانتفاء شرط وقوع الفعل، يصح التكليف به اتفاقاً.

كشيد أمر عبده بخياطة ثوب غدا وهو جاهل عن بقاء حياة العبد إلى الغد. ولما اعترض ابن الهمام في التحرير وقال: وقد تقدم (في الباب الثالث في مسألة امتناع التكليف بالمحال) أن الإجماع منعقد على صحة التكليف بما علم الله أنه لا يقع من المكلف (كأيمان أبي جهل) ومعلوم أن كل ما لا يقع من المكلف فإما يكون عدم وقوعه بانتفاء شرط (وقوعه) من إرادة قديمة (هي إرادة الله تعالى) أي لم يرد الله وقوعه، وإما من إرادة حادثة (وهي إرادة العبد) أي لم يرده العبد. فحكاية الخلاف بين الجمهور وبين المعتزلة في تكليف ما لا يوجد شرط وقوعه عند وقته مناقضة وإبطال لهذا الإجماع، فأجاب عنه المصنف وقال: لا يقال: (هكذا) لأنا نقول في الجواب إن ذلك الإجماع (على صحة التكليف بما علم الله أنه لا يقع) كان بالنظر إلى الإمكان الذاتي، أي ذلك التكليف ممكن في حد ذاته (كما يدل عليه) على أن المراد بالصحة الإمكان الذاتي كلام بعض المحققين عند نقل الإجماع على صحة هذا التكليف) حيث قال: وإن ظن قوم أنه التكليف بما علم الله أنه لا يقع من فلان (ممتنع لغيره) (أي بسبب علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه) فالخلاف هنا (بين الجمهور والمعتزلة) في وقوع ذلك التكليف عملاً ووجود مصداقه، بعد الاتفاق على إمكانه الذاتي وصحته صحة ذاتية، أي بعد الاتفاق على إمكان ذلك التكليف إمكاناً ذاتياً وصحته في حد ذاته.

دليل الجمهور على جواز التكليف بالفعل الذي علم الأمر انتفاء شرط وقوعه: والدليل لنا (أنه) لم يصح التكليف بذلك الفعل الذي لم يعلم أحد أنه مكلف به قبل وقت الفعل (فلا يتهيأ له الجواز أن لا يوجد شرط من شروط وقوعه) وقد اجتهدت جماعة ليجيبوا عن هذا الدليل، فأشار المصنف إليه بقوله: وقد أنكر قوم العلم بالتكليف بفعل ما قبل وقته (أي وقت الفعل) أي لا يكون ضرورة العلم بالتكليف ضرورياً قبل وقت الفعل. ثم أجاب عن هذا الإنكار بقوله: وذلك (الإنكار) باطل؛ للإجماع على تحقق الفعل، أي تحقق وجوبه على المكلف قبل التمكن، أي قبل كون المكلف قادراً على إيجاد الفعل، بدليل وجوب الشروع بنية أداء الواجب من الصوم أو الصلاة إجماعاً، وهو أي وجوب الشروع فرع تحقق الوجوب، ويتحقق الوجوب بالعلم، فلابد أولاً من العلم بوجوب الواجب قبل أدائه في وقته.

دليل المعتزلة من وجهين على عدم جواز التكليف بذلك الفعل:
1 - قالوا: أولاً: لو صح التكليف بالفعل الذي عَلِمَ الأمر انتفاء شرط وقوعه عند وقته لزم أن لا يكون الإمكان شرطاً في التكليف؛ لأن ما عَدِمَ شرط من شروطه فهو غير ممكن (والحال) أن الإمكان شرط التكليف، فردّ المصنف دليلهم بوجهين: الأول: قلنا: الشرط في التكليف هو الإمكان العادي (دون الذاتي) وهو لا ينافي الامتناع لغيره (وهو عدم وجود شرط الوقوع عند وقته).
They said first: That whose condition is absent is impossible, and possibility is a condition of obligation. We say: The condition is customary possibility, and it does not contradict impossibility due to another. Also it is refuted by the ignorance of the commander regarding the absence of the condition in reality, for knowledge has no bearing on possibility and impossibility, as it follows the known. Secondly: If it were valid with the knowledge of the commander, it would be valid with the knowledge of the commanded, because the non-occurrence is common, and the necessary consequence is false by agreement. We say: Rather due to the absence of benefit.

An act that is possible whose conditions for its necessary existence are complete, when the commander (the Lawgiver) knows the absence of a condition of its occurrence at its time, is the command valid? The majority said: It is valid, contrary to the Mu'tazila and Imam al-Haramayn. The dispute is only regarding the condition of occurrence that is not a condition for the obligation of the act, for when the condition of its obligation is absent or when the commander knows the absence of the condition of obligation, the command does not occur, for command cannot be conceived without obligation necessarily. In the case where the commander does not know the absence of the condition of occurrence, the command is valid by agreement.

... The evidence of the majority for the permissibility of commanding the act whose condition of occurrence the commander knows to be absent: And the evidence for us is that the command for that act which no one knows he is commanded before the time of the act is not valid, so there is no preparation for it that a condition of its occurrence might be absent. And a group exerted effort to answer this evidence, so the author pointed to it by saying: Some people denied knowledge of the command for an act before its time, meaning the necessity of knowledge of the command is not necessary before the time of the act. Then he answered this denial by saying: That denial is false, due to consensus on the realization of the act, i.e. the realization of its obligation upon the obligated before ability, as evidenced by the obligation to begin with the intention of performing the obligation of fasting or prayer by consensus, and the obligation to begin is a branch of the realization of the obligation, and the obligation is realized by knowledge, so knowledge of the obligation is necessary first before its performance in its time.

Evidence of the Mu'tazila from two aspects for the impermissibility of commanding that act:
1 - They said: First: If the command for the act whose condition of occurrence the commander knows to be absent at its time were valid, it would necessitate that possibility is not a condition in the command, because that which lacks a condition of its conditions is impossible, while possibility is a condition of the command. The author refuted their evidence in two ways: The first: We say: The condition in the command is customary possibility (not essential), and it does not contradict impossibility due to another (which is the absence of the condition of occurrence at its time).
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a structured usul dispute between the majority (Hanafis, Ash'aris) and the Mu'tazila regarding the validity of a command (taklif) when the Lawgiver knows that a condition of the occurrence of the commanded act will be absent at its time. The author, al-Badakhshani, refines earlier discussions by Ibn al-Humam and responds to objections.

The passage first presents the Mu'tazila's initial argument: if the condition is absent, the act is impossible, and possibility is a condition of obligation; therefore the command is invalid. The author refutes by distinguishing between essential possibility (imkan dhati) and customary possibility (imkan 'adi). Customary possibility refers to acts that are normally possible, even if some external condition is absent. This distinction resolves the issue.

A second Mu'tazili argument claims that if the commander's knowledge of the condition's absence makes the command invalid, then the commanded's knowledge should also make it invalid, but the commanded does not know. The author says the difference is the benefit (fā'ida) — the commanded may still strive.

The author then defines the precise scope of the dispute: it is only about a condition of occurrence (shart al-wuqoo') that is not a condition of obligation (shart al-wujub). If the condition of obligation is absent, there is no obligation and thus no valid command. But if the condition of occurrence is absent while the condition of obligation is present, the command can be valid.

Ibn al-Humam objected that this contradicts the consensus (ijma) on the validity of commanding what Allah knows will not occur (e.g., the oaths of Abu Jahl). The author responds by clarifying that the consensus refers to essential possibility, while the current dispute concerns actual occurrence (wuqoo' 'amalan) — i.e., whether the command is actualized in practice. All agree on essential possibility; the dispute is over whether such a command can occur as a real obligation.

The majority then provides positive evidence for their position: obligation is realized before the ability to perform, as proven by the obligation to begin with intention (niyya) for prayer and fasting before the actual action. This obligation to start shows that knowledge of the obligation must exist before the act, indicating that the command is valid even if the condition of occurrence is not yet met.

Finally, the Mu'tazila's second round of argument is presented and refuted: they argue that if such a command were valid, possibility would not be a condition. The author repeats the distinction: the condition is customary possibility, not essential. Customary possibility is not contradicted by impossibility due to another (i.e., the absence of the condition of occurrence).

Key Terms
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkan al-dhati): Essential possibility: the act is possible in itself, regardless of external conditions or knowledge.
الإمكان العادي (al-imkan al-'adi): Customary possibility: the act is normally possible according to the usual course of events, even if a condition of occurrence is absent in a particular case.
شرط الوقوع (shart al-wuqoo'): Condition of occurrence: a condition that must be present for the act to actually happen at its appointed time, but not necessarily for the obligation.
شرط الوجوب (shart al-wujub): Condition of obligation: a condition whose presence is necessary for the command to impose an obligation. If absent, there is no obligation and no valid command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that the author uses to reconcile the majority's view with the consensus on commanding what Allah knows will not occur?
A) Distinction between essential possibility and customary possibility
B) Distinction between obligation and recommendation
C) Distinction between knowledge of the commander and knowledge of the commanded
D) Distinction between condition of obligation and condition of occurrence

Key Takeaway
The validity of a divine command requires only essential possibility (imkan dhati), not customary possibility (imkan 'adi); thus a command remains valid even when Allah knows its condition of occurrence will be absent.

The Validity of Taklif When God Knows a Condition Will Be Missing
Dispute on validity of taklif with known missing condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of taklif when the commander (God) knows a condition will be absent, and refute the Mu'tazila arguments using the distinction between subjective and intrinsic possibility.

Core Idea
Taklif is valid based on ordinary possibility, not actual occurrence; God's knowledge does not change what is possible.

This passage tackles a subtle usul al-fiqh dispute: can Allah command an action that He knows will never occur due to a missing condition? The Mu'tazila argue no, because it would be commanding an impossibility. The Hanafis and majority affirm its validity, grounding their position in the distinction between intrinsic and ordinary possibility, and the benefit of trial (ibtilāʾ). In this lesson, you will dissect both sides of the argument and understand the Hanafi reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine what exactly is being disputed: the validity of taklif (obligation) concerning an act that the commander (God) knows will not occur due to a missing condition at its time.
Both sides agree the act is intrinsically possible (imkān dhātī).
2
State the agreed premise
Recognize that the dispute is not about intrinsic possibility but about actual validity (wuqūʿ). The majority (Hanafis) and Mu'tazila agree that such taklif is possible in itself, but disagree on whether it can actually be valid as a command.
The disagreement is over 'waqūʿ' (actual occurrence) of the obligation, not its essential possibility.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the majority's evidence: consensus that obligation is established before ability (tamakkun). The obligation to begin an obligatory act with intention (e.g., fasting or prayer) before being able to perform it shows that knowledge of the obligation precedes the act's occurrence.
The proof is based on ijmāʿ (consensus) that one must start an act with intention before having full ability, indicating wujūb exists prior to actual performance.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazila first argument and Hanafi response
The Mu'tazila argue that if such taklif were valid, then possibility would not be a condition for taklif, because an act lacking a condition is impossible. The Hanafi response distinguishes ordinary possibility (imkān ʿādī) from intrinsic possibility (imkān dhātī). Ordinary possibility is the relevant condition, and it is not negated by impossibility due to an external missing condition. Additionally, the argument is refuted by cases where a human commander is ignorant of the missing condition yet taklif is valid by consensus.
Knowledge follows the known; it does not affect the reality of possibility.
5
Analyze the Mu'tazila second argument and Hanafi response
The Mu'tazila claim that if the commander's knowledge does not prevent validity, then the subject's knowledge should also not prevent it, leading to inconsistency. The Hanafi response states that the preventer in the subject's case is the absence of benefit (fāʾida). For the subject, knowing the act cannot occur makes obligation futile (no compliance or trial). However, when only the commander knows, there is still the benefit of trial (ibtilāʾ) to test the subject's resolve.
Thus, the distinction is based on the presence or absence of benefit, not on the non-occurrence of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وإن ظن قوم أنه التكليف بما علم الله أنه لا يقع من فلان ( ممتنع لغيره (أي بسبب علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه) فالخلاف هنا ( بين الجمهور والمعتزله ) في وقوع ذلك التكليف عملاً ووجود مصداقه، بعد الاتفاق على إمكانه الذاتي وصحته صحة ذاتية، أى بعد الاتفاق على إمكان ذلك التكليف إمكانا ذاتياً وصحنه في حد ذاته.
دليل الجمهور على جواز التكليف بالفعل الذى علم الأمر انتفاء شرط وقوعه
والدليل ) لنا ( أنه) لم يصح التكليف بذلك الفعل الذي لم يعلم أحد أنه مكلف به قبل وقت الفعل ( فلا يتهيأ له الجواز أن لا يوجد شرط من شروط وقوعه) وقد اجتهدت جماعة ليجيبوا عن هذ الدليل، فأشار المصنف إليه بقوله : وقد أنكر قوم العلم بالتكليف بفعل ما ) قبل وقته (أي وقت الفعل) أي لا يكون ضرورة العلم بالتكليف ضرورياً قبل وقت الفعل.
ثم أجاب عن هذا الإنكار بقوله : وذلك (الإنكار) باطل؛ للإجماع على تحقق الفعل، أي تحقق وجوبه على المكلف قبل التمكن ، أي قبل كون المكلف قادراً على إيجاد الفعل ، بدليل وجوب الشروع بنية أداء الواجب من الصوم أو الصلاة) إجماعاً، وهو أي وجوب الشروع فرع تحقق الوجوب ، ويتحقق الوجوب بالعلم، فلابد أولاً من العلم بوجوب الواجب قبل أدائه في وقته.
دليل المعتزلة من وجهين على عدم جواز التكليف بذلك الفعل
۱ - قالوا :أولاً: لو صح التكليف بالفعل الذى عَلِمَ الأمر انتفاء شرط وقوعه عند وقته لزم أن لا يكون الإمكان شرطا في التكليف؛ لأن ما عَدِمَ شرط من شروطه فهو غير ممكن (والحال) أن الإمكان شرط التكليف، فردّ المصنف دليلهم بوجهين:
الأول : قلنا : الشرط في التكليف هو الإمكان العادى (دون الذاتي ) وهو لا ينافي الامتناع لغيره ( وهو عدم وجود شرط الوقوع عند وقته ).
والثاني: أن هذ الدليل أيضا منقوض يجهل الأمر (إذا كان إنساناً) لعدم وجود الشرط في الواقع، بأن يكون شرط الوقوع معدوماً في الواقع، ولا يعلمه الآمر . فماكان شرط وقوعه معدوماً في الواقع ، وكان الأمر جاهلاً عن عدمه، فهو غير ممكن في الواقع، فقد فات شرط التكليف، ونقض الذليل فيلزم أن لا يصح التكليف به مع أنه صح اتفاقاً، لأنه لا دخل لعلم الأمر بوجود شرط الوقوع ( و إمكان (الفعل) ولا لعلمه بعدم ذلك الشرط وامتناع الفعل؛ فإن العلم تابع للمعلوم فإن كان المعلوم ممكن فعلمه بصفة الإمكان، وان كان ممتنعاً فعلمه بصفة الامتناع.
٢ - وقالوا ثانياً: لوصح التكليف بالفعل الذى علم الأمر انتفاء شرط وقوعه عند ،وقته لصح التكليف به، مع علم المأمور المكلف بانتفاء شرط ،وقوعه، لأن المانع من عدم صحة التكليف بالفعل الذي علم المأمور انتفاء شرط وقوعه لا يتصور من غير عدم حصول الفعل في الواقع، وعدم حصول الفعل مشترك بين علم الأمر بانتفاء الشرط ، وبين علم المأمور بانتفائه، مع أن علم الأمر بانتفاء الشرط مانع عن التكليف دون علم المأمور باتنفاء الشرط ، ولكن يلزم من قولكم إن علم المأمور باتنفاء الشرط أيضاً مانع عن التكليف واللازم وهو صحة التكليف بذلك الفعل مع علم المأمور بانتفاء شرطه باطل اتفاقاً، إذ لا يصح التكليف مع علم المأمور بعدم شرط وقوعه. قلنا في الجواب عن الدليل الثاني : إنا لا نسلم أن المانع عن صحة التكليف على تقدير علم المأمور بعدم الشرط هو عدم حصول الفعل ، بل المانع عن صحة التكليف في صورة علم المأمور بعدم شرطه هو انتفاء الفائدة من التكليف ، فإنّ الفائدة من التكليف إما الامتثال أو الابتلاء بالعزم على الطاعة ، وفي صورة علم الأمر باتنفاء الشرط تكون الفائدة هي الابتلاء هل يعزم المكلف الطاعة أم لا؟
He said: And if some people think that the obligation (taklif) regarding that which God knows will not occur from so-and-so is impossible due to other (i.e., due to God's knowledge of its non-occurrence), then the disagreement here (between the majority and the Mu'tazila) concerns the actual occurrence of that obligation and the existence of its instance, after agreement on its intrinsic possibility and its validity in essence; that is, after agreement on the intrinsic possibility of that obligation and its validity in itself.
The proof of the majority for the permissibility of obligation concerning an act whose condition the commander knows to be absent:
And the proof (for us) is that (if) the obligation concerning that act which no one knew he was obligated to do before the time of the act were not valid, then it would not be possible for him that a condition of its occurrence does not exist? Some people exerted effort to respond to this proof, so the author pointed to it by saying: Some people denied the knowledge of obligation concerning an act before its time (i.e., the time of the act), meaning that the necessity of knowing the obligation is not necessary before the time of the act.
Then he responded to this denial by saying: That (denial) is false because of consensus on the realization of the act, i.e., the realization of its obligation upon the obligated person before his ability (tamakkun), i.e., before the obligated person is capable of bringing about the act, as evidenced by the obligation to begin with the intention of performing the obligatory act of fasting or prayer, by consensus. And this obligation to begin is a branch of the existence of obligation, and obligation is realized by knowledge, so first there must be knowledge of the obligatory act before its performance in its time.
The proof of the Mu'tazila, from two aspects, for the impermissibility of obligation concerning that act:
1- They said: First, if the obligation concerning an act whose condition of occurrence the commander knows to be absent at its time were valid, then possibility would not be a condition for obligation; because what lacks a condition of its conditions is impossible (and the case is that) possibility is a condition for obligation. So the author refuted their proof in two ways: The first: We say: The condition for obligation is ordinary possibility (al-imkan al-'adi) (not intrinsic possibility), and it does not contradict impossibility due to other (which is the absence of the condition of occurrence at its time). And the second: That this proof is also invalidated by the commander's ignorance (if he is a human) of the absence of the condition in reality, such that the condition of occurrence is non-existent in reality and the commander does not know it. So what has its condition of occurrence non-existent in reality while the commander is ignorant of its non-existence is impossible in reality, so the condition of obligation has been missed, and the proof is invalidated; thus it would follow that obligation concerning it is not valid, while it is valid by agreement, because the commander's knowledge of the existence of the condition of occurrence (and the possibility of the act) nor his knowledge of the non-existence of that condition and the impossibility of the act have no effect; for knowledge follows the known: if the known is possible, his knowledge has the attribute of possibility, and if it is impossible, his knowledge has the attribute of impossibility.
2- And they said, second: If the obligation concerning an act whose condition of occurrence the commander knows to be absent at its time were valid, then obligation concerning it would be valid also with the obligated person's knowledge of the absence of its condition of occurrence, because the preventer from the invalidity of obligation concerning an act whose condition of occurrence the obligated person knows to be absent cannot be imagined apart from the non-occurrence of the act in reality, and the non-occurrence of the act is shared between the commander's knowledge of the absence of the condition and the obligated person's knowledge of its absence, yet the commander's knowledge of the absence of the condition is a preventer of obligation, while the obligated person's knowledge of the absence of the condition is not? But it would follow from your statement that the obligated person's knowledge of the absence of the condition is also a preventer of obligation, and the necessary consequence—which is the validity of obligation concerning that act with the obligated person's knowledge of the absence of its condition—is false by agreement, for obligation is not valid with the obligated person's knowledge of the non-existence of its condition of occurrence. We say in response to the second proof: We do not concede that the preventer of the validity of obligation, assuming the obligated person's knowledge of the non-existence of the condition, is the non-occurrence of the act; rather, the preventer of the validity of obligation in the case of the obligated person's knowledge of the non-existence of its condition is the absence of benefit (fāʾida) from the obligation. For the benefit of obligation is either compliance (imtithāl) or trial by resolve (ibtilāʾ bi al-ʿazm ʿalā al-ṭāʿa). And in the case of the commander's knowledge of the absence of the condition, the benefit is the trial: will the obligated person resolve to be obedient or not?
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents a usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the validity of taklif (obligation) when the commander (God) knows that a necessary condition for the act's occurrence will be absent at its time. The majority (Ahl al-Sunna) hold that such taklif is valid in actuality, while the Mu'tazila deny its validity. The author first states the agreed premise: the taklif is intrinsically possible. Then he gives the majority's proof: knowledge of the obligation must exist before the time of the act, proven by the consensus (ijma) that one must begin an obligatory act (like fasting or prayer) with intention before one is able to perform it—this shows obligation exists prior to ability. The Mu'tazila raise two arguments against validity. First, they argue that if such taklif were valid, then possibility (imkan) would not be a condition for taklif, because an act lacking a condition is impossible. The author refutes this by distinguishing between subjective possibility (imkan 'adi, meaning possibility in the normal course) and intrinsic possibility (imkan dhati). Subjective possibility is the relevant condition, and it is not negated by the external impossibility due to a missing condition. Additionally, the proof is refuted by cases where the human commander is ignorant of the missing condition, yet taklif is valid by consensus even though the act is objectively impossible. This shows that the commander's knowledge does not affect the reality—knowledge is subordinate to the known. Second, the Mu'tazila argue that if the commander's knowledge of the missing condition does not prevent validity, then the subject's knowledge of the missing condition should also not prevent it, leading to a contradiction. The author responds that the preventer in the subject's case is the absence of benefit (fāʾida), not the non-occurrence of the act. For the subject, knowing the act cannot occur makes the obligation futile (no benefit of compliance or trial). However, when only the commander knows (but the subject does not), there is still benefit: the trial of whether the subject will resolve to obey despite his ignorance. This distinction shows why taklif is valid in the latter case.

Key Terms
التكليف (al-taklīf): The imposition of a duty (obligation) upon a responsible person by the Lawgiver.
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkān al-dhātī): Intrinsic possibility — an act is possible in itself, regardless of external conditions.
الإمكان العادي (al-imkān al-ʿādī): Ordinary or subjective possibility — possibility according to normal circumstances and conditions.
الامتناع لغيره (al-imtināʿ li-ghayrihi): Impossibility due to an external factor (e.g., missing condition), not impossibility in itself.
التمكن (al-tamakkun): Ability or capacity to perform an act; having the power and opportunity to execute the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis in the text, why is taklif valid even when God knows a condition will be missing?
A) Because intrinsic possibility is sufficient.
B) Because the condition for taklif is ordinary possibility, and God's knowledge does not negate it.
C) Because the act is actually possible.
D) Because the commander's ignorance makes it valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position affirms the validity of taklif with a known missing condition by distinguishing ordinary possibility from intrinsic possibility and emphasizing the benefit of trial.

Minor's Capacity in Divorce and Commutative Contracts
Textual analysis of minor's capacity in fifth and sixth action types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two categories of actions a minor cannot perform even with guardian permission (divorce-like acts) and can perform with guardian permission (commutative contracts), and evaluate the internal Hanafi dispute regarding disadvantageous sales.

Core Idea
Divorce is tied to ownership of marriage, not permission; sale requires guardian to neutralize risk of harm.

This lesson examines the fifth and sixth types of actions in the classification of a minor's capacity. The fifth includes unilateral repudiations like divorce, which a minor can never perform even with guardian consent. The sixth includes commutative contracts like sale, where guardian permission enables the minor to transact, but a critical dispute arises over whether a minor can sell at a disadvantage to strangers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The passage delineates fifth (divorce-like) and sixth (commutative contracts) types. Name each and their key characteristics: the fifth type is absolutely prohibited even with permission; the sixth type is permissible with permission but subject to conditions.
The fifth type includes divorce and other unilateral ikhtiyārāt; the sixth includes sale, lease, etc.
2
Analyze the rationale for the fifth type
Understand why divorce is completely unavailable to a minor even with permission. The text quotes al-Sarakhsī refuting a mistaken view that a minor's divorce is entirely invalid. The correct view: divorce is owned by owning the marriage (milk al-nikāh), and the minor lacks that ownership; thus permission cannot grant it. However, if needed to repel harm, it could be valid.
Al-Sarakhsī's statement clarifies that the ruling is not fundamentally invalid but tied to ownership.
3
Examine the dispute on the sixth type
Identify the positions of Abū Ḥanīfa and the two disciples (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) regarding a disadvantageous sale (bay' fāḥish) with strangers. Abū Ḥanīfa holds that permission compensates for deficiency, making the minor like an adult. The disciples hold that permission does not fully eliminate the risk of harm.
The disciples' view is deemed 'more apparent' (أظهر) in the text.
4
Determine the tarjih
The text states 'their statement is more apparent' (وقولهما أظهر), indicating a preference for the view of the two disciples. This means the operative ruling is that a minor cannot engage in a disadvantageous sale with strangers even with guardian permission.
This is a clear tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبد فيجب له الأجرة بشرط السلامة، فلو هلك فالقيمة، لا الأجر، واستحق الرضخ مع عدم جواز شهود القتل بدون الإذن بالإجماع والخامس كالطلاق ونحوه فلا يملكه ولو بإذن وليه، كما لا يملكه عليه غيره قال شمس الأئمة السرخسى زعم بعض مشايخنا، أن هذا الحكم غير مشروع اصلاً ، حتى أن امرأته لاتكون محلاً للطلاق، وهذا ،وهم، فإن الطلاق يملك بملك النكاح ، ولا ضرر فيه، وإنما هو في الإيقاع، فلو تحققت الحاجة إليه لدفع الضرر لكان صحيحاً، وإنما يجوز إقراض القاضى ماله من الملى؛ لأنه حفظ مع قدرة الاقتضاء بعلمه، بخلاف الأب، إلا في رواية والسادس كالبيع والإجارة وغيرهما من المعاوضات، ففيها نفع مشوب باحتمال ضرر، فبانضمام رأى الولى يندفع الاحتمال، فيملكه معه ثم عند أبي حنيفة لما انجبر القصور بالإذن كان كالبالغ، فيملك بيع فاحش مع الأجانب والولى في رواية، وعندهما لا يجوز، وقولهما أظهر
The slave deserves the wage on condition of safety; if it perishes, the value, not the wage. And he is entitled to a share [from the spoils] despite the impermissibility of testifying for killing without permission by consensus. The fifth [type] is like divorce and its like: he does not own it even with the permission of his guardian, just as no one else owns it for him. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: 'Some of our shaykhs claimed that this ruling is not legislated at all, such that his wife is not a locus for divorce. But this is a mistake; for divorce is owned by owning the marriage, and there is no harm in it, it is only in the initiation. If the need for it arises to repel harm, it would be valid.' And a judge may lend his property to a wealthy person because he preserves it with the ability to reclaim due to his knowledge, unlike the father, except in one narration. The sixth [type] is like sale and lease and other commutative contracts: in them there is benefit mixed with possibility of harm, so with the joining of the guardian's opinion the possibility is repelled, so he owns it with him. Then according to Abu Hanifa, since the deficiency is compensated by permission, he is like an adult, so he owns an excessively disadvantageous sale with strangers and the guardian in one narration. According to the two [disciples], it is not permissible, and their statement is more apparent.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the capacity of a minor (and in the first part, a slave) to perform certain legal acts. The author explains the fifth and sixth types of actions in the classification of what a minor can or cannot do. The fifth type includes divorce (ṭalāq) and similar unilateral repudiations; a minor cannot perform them even with the guardian's permission because the right to divorce is tied to ownership of the marriage, which the minor lacks. Al-Sarakhsī refutes a mistaken view that a minor's divorce is entirely invalid, arguing that if needed to repel harm, it could be valid. The sixth type includes commutative contracts like sale and lease; these involve benefit mixed with potential harm, so a guardian's opinion neutralizes the risk, allowing the minor to contract with the guardian. There is a disagreement: Abū Ḥanīfa holds that with permission the minor becomes fully capable, even for an excessively disadvantageous sale with strangers, while the two disciples (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) deny this, and their view is deemed stronger (أظهر). The initial part about the slave's wage and share of spoils appears to be part of the same discussion on capacity (perhaps the first category).

Key Terms
الإجارة (al-ijāra): A commutative contract (hire/lease) where benefit is exchanged for payment.
البيع الفاحش (al-bayʿ al-fāḥish): A sale that is clearly disadvantageous or excessive in price imbalance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a minor performing a divorce (talāq) with the permission of his guardian?
A) It is valid because the guardian's permission compensates for the minor's deficiency.
B) It is valid only if needed to repel harm.
C) It is not valid; the minor does not own divorce even with permission.
D) It is valid but requires court approval.

Key Takeaway
The classification of a minor's actions hinges on the nature of the act: unilateral repudiations like divorce require personal ownership, while commutative contracts require guardian oversight to neutralize harm.

Apostasy of a Minor: Hanafi Istihsan vs. Darar Mahd
Explains the ruling on a minor's apostasy and its consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the apostasy of a minor using the principle of istihsan and distinguish it from the opposing view of al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A minor's apostasy has worldly consequences by istihsan; the severity of kufr overrides the excuse of minority.

This passage addresses a key dispute in Hanafi usul regarding the legal effect of a minor's apostasy. While some scholars argue that a minor's actions are not fully consequential, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule by istihsan that apostasy is valid and affects marriage and inheritance. Understanding this dispute refines our grasp of how legal capacity (ahliyyah) interacts with the absolute prohibition of disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Positions
List the scholars involved: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold one view; Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold the opposing view. Note the difference in worldly vs. afterlife rulings.
All agree on afterlife consequences (e.g., no funeral prayer).
2
Locate the Hanafi Proof
Find the statement that disbelief is absolutely prohibited (al-kufr mahzur mutlaqan). This is the basis for the Hanafi ruling by istihsan.
This proof is a general maxim, not a specific textual evidence.
3
Understand the Role of Istihsan
Istihsan is used to depart from the general rule that minors' purely harmful acts are ineffective. The severity of kufr justifies this departure.
Istihsan here overrides the default qiyas that would treat minor's apostasy like other harmful acts.
4
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under the Hanafi view, the minor's marriage dissolves and inheritance is lost; under the opposing view, these do not occur.
This is the concrete outcome of the usul dispute.
5
Examine the Execution Exception
Even though apostasy is considered, the minor is not killed because execution requires muharaba (waging war) and there is doubt (shubha) about the validity of a minor's Islam.
This shows that not all effects of apostasy apply to minors.

Because of his apostasy; for it is pure harm. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf said likewise, but his apostasy is valid (i.e., his apostasy is considered by the proof of istihsan according to us (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). (This difference is in considering the apostasy of the minor and its non-consideration in the worldly rulings of inheritance, marriage, etc.). As for the rulings of the Hereafter, the apostasy of the minor is valid, i.e., it is considered by agreement, such that if the minor apostate dies, he is not prayed upon by consensus. The face of istihsan and its proof: that disbelief is prohibited absolutely, whether it emanates from a sane adult or from a minor. So disbelief is not dropped and is not excused by an unheeded excuse, namely his being a subject of mercy due to minority. For this complete wretchedness (the wretchedness of disbelief) removes him from being a subject of mercy. Therefore, due to the consideration of his apostasy, his wife becomes irrevocably divorced, and he is barred from inheriting from his relatives by apostasy. However, the minor is not killed for apostasy (despite the consideration of his apostasy); rather he is restrained and imprisoned because (the obligation of killing) is not merely for apostasy, but for war (muharaba) against Allah and His Messenger after apostasy, and the minor is not among the people of war. Nor is the apostate minor killed after reaching adulthood (either) because the validity of a minor's Islam is disputed among scholars: some say his Islam is valid, some say it is not, thus creating doubt (shubha) regarding the permissibility of killing him.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on a minor's apostasy, part of a discussion on legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author presents an ikhtilaf: According to Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf, a minor's apostasy is not considered in worldly matters because it is pure harm (darar mahd) and a minor is not fully responsible. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan rule by istihsan that the apostasy is valid in worldly matters such as inheritance and marriage: the wife becomes divorced, and the minor is barred from inheriting. In afterlife rulings, all agree that the minor dies as a disbeliever and funeral prayer is not offered. The proof for istihsan is that disbelief is absolutely prohibited regardless of age; a minor's excuse of being a subject of mercy does not remove the severity of disbelief. However, the minor is not executed because execution is for muharaba (waging war), not merely apostasy, and a minor is not capable of muharaba. Additionally, there is doubt (shubha) regarding the validity of a minor's Islam, which prevents capital punishment.

Key Terms
ضرر محض (darar mahd): Pure harm: an action that brings only detriment and no benefit.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that departs from strict analogy (qiyas) for a stronger legal reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The minor is only punished after puberty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, by istihsan, holds that a minor's apostasy has worldly consequences (divorce, loss of inheritance) because the absolute prohibition of disbelief overrides the excuse of minority, though execution is not carried out.

License of Shortening Prayer Applies Even in Sinful Travel
Hanafi argument for qasr in sinful travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school maintains that the license to shorten prayer applies during sinful travel, and critically evaluate the opposing argument using the principle of adjacency (mujawara).

Core Idea
The license to shorten prayer is attached to travel itself, not to the traveler's righteousness; sin is adjacent but not part of the cause.

This lesson examines a classical usul dispute: does the license (rukhsa) to shorten prayer (qasr) apply when travel itself is sinful? The Hanafi school argues that it does, because the textual proofs are absolute and the sin is external to the cause of the license. The three other Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) maintain that a license is a blessing that cannot be attained through sin. By analyzing the Hanafi response—which distinguishes between the cause (safar) and an associated sin (ma'siya)—you will understand a key usul principle about how causes operate in the presence of sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi dalil
Locate the Quranic verse and hadith cited by the Hanafis. Note that the word 'safar' is used absolutely without restriction.
The Hanafi position relies on the literal, unqualified text.
2
Understand the opposing argument
The three Imams argue that a license is a blessing (ni'mah) and cannot be obtained through sin. They analogize with intoxication which negates legal capacity.
Their analogy is based on the nature of the cause being sinful in itself.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The sin is adjacent (mujawir) to the travel, not part of it. The traveler has two attributes: traveling (cause) and robbery (sin). The license attaches to the cause.
This is the principle of adjacency (mujawara).
4
Apply the analogy with prayer in usurped land
The Hanafis compare this to praying in usurped land: the prayer itself is obedience, while the usurpation is an external sin. The blessing of reward remains.
This shows that the concomitant sin does not nullify the license attached to the primary act.
5
Evaluate the strength of the Hanafi position
The author notes that the interpretation might seem strange, but the principle of textual absoluteness and cause-separation is maintained.
Consider the role of intention (niyya) as a counterpoint.

Prayer on the tongue of your Prophet: in residence four, and in travel two. Our proof: the absolute wording of the license, like His statement (And whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days) and Allah did not restrict travel to travel of righteousness or lack of sin. And in Sahih Muslim from Ibn Abbas: 'Allah prescribed prayer on the tongue of your Prophet: in residence four, and in travel two.' And in the hadith the word 'travel' is absolute. The proof of the three Imams: They said: A license is a blessing, so it cannot be attained through sin like intoxication. We said: It (the travel) is not sin, but adjacent to it, so it becomes like prayer in usurped land, unlike the cause of sin itself like intoxication through drinking prohibited intoxicant. They said: A license is a blessing, so it cannot be attained through sin, due to the loss of intellect through intoxication, for it (intoxication) is not pardoned nor a license; rather, rulings follow from it such as occurrence of divorce, emancipation, and liability for damaging others' property, unlike loss of intellect due to insanity or fainting, which is pardoned and a license, and no rulings attach. The author said in response to their proof: We said: Sin is not the travel, rather sin is adjacent to travel, external to it. Thus the traveling highway robber has two attributes: one is covering distance from place to place, the second is waging war against Allah and His messenger by highway robbery, plunder, and banditry. Sin is the second, not the first. The cause for attaining the blessing is the first, i.e., travel itself. (This is a strange interpretation, for actions are by intentions, and if there were no travel there would be no highway robbery and corruption). Then he analogized this travel to prayer in usurped land, saying: Like prayer in usurped land, for it is in itself obedience and not sin, being a foundational act of worship in the religion, while occupying another's property without permission is adjacent to the prayer and is sin. Thus prayer in itself necessitates the blessing of reward, unlike occupying another's property without permission, unlike the cause that is sin in itself, like intoxication resulting from drinking prohibited intoxicant, and occupation of usurped land occurs through prayer which is an act of worship.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) regarding the application of the license (rukhsa) to shorten prayer during travel (qasr) when the travel itself is sinful (e.g., highway robbery). The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and his companions, is that the license applies regardless of the sinfulness of the journey, because the textual proofs (Quran and hadith) are absolute regarding travel. The author first states the Hanafi proof: the Quranic verse 'if you are on a journey' is unqualified, and the hadith of Ibn Abbas uses the absolute word 'travel'. The three imams argue that a license is a blessing (ni'mah) and cannot be obtained through sin, drawing an analogy with intoxication (sukr), which is a cause of sin and thus nullifies one's legal capacity. They further argue that public decapacitation (zawal al-'aql) due to intoxication is not a license or pardon, unlike insanity. The Hanafi response, attributed to the author, distinguishes between the act of travel itself and the accompanying sin. The traveler who is a highway robber has two attributes: the act of traveling (covering distance) which is the cause of the license, and the act of robbery which is sin and external to the travel. They analogize this to prayer in usurped land: the prayer itself is obedience, even though the occupation of the land is sin. The blessing of reward for prayer is not negated by the sin of usurpation. Similarly, the license for qasr is attached to the travel itself, not to the sin. The Hanafi position thus relies on the principle that the cause (sabab) of the ruling is the travel itself, and the sin is merely concurrent (mujawir) but not part of the cause.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsa): A dispensation or license that eases the original obligation due to a valid excuse.
السفر (al-safar): Travel; the act of covering distance from one place to another.
معصية (ma'siya): Sin; disobedience to Allah's commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the sin is adjacent to the travel, not part of its cause.
C) Because the three imams agree with the Hanafi view.
D) Because the hadith specifically mentions sinful travel.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the application of the license to shorten prayer during travel regardless of the sinfulness of the journey, based on the principle that the cause (safar) is distinct from the concomitant sin.

Accountability for Error: A Hanafi Usul Dispute with the Mu'tazila
Hanafi defense of rational permissibility of punishing error

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Mu'tazili positions on accountability for error, evaluate the Hanafi proof from Quran 2:286, and apply the principle of shubha to distinguish between penal and civil liability.

Core Idea
Accountability for error is rationally possible, but shubha waives hadd and qisas while preserving civil liability.

This lesson explores a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafi school and the Mu'tazila: is it rationally permissible for Allah to hold a person accountable for an act committed by mistake (khata')? The Hanafi position, as argued by the author, affirms rational permissibility, grounding it in a Quranic verse that implies accountability is possible. The Mu'tazila object, claiming accountability requires intent. The Hanafi response introduces a nuanced distinction: error involves negligence (tark al-tathabbut), which constitutes a delict, but a semblance of doubt (shubha) drops penal sanctions while allowing civil liability. The same reasoning extends to divorce by an erroneous utterance, leading to a further intra-Sunni disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and the opposing view
Identify the core claim: the Hanafis hold that holding someone accountable for error in this world is rationally permissible. The Mu'tazila deny this. Note the practical consequence: if accountability were impossible, then even civil liability could not be imposed.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from Quran 2:286
Examine the verse 'Our Lord, do not hold us to account if we forget or err.' The Hanafi argument: asking for exemption from something impossible is invalid. Therefore, accountability for error must be rationally possible. Discuss the dalala (zanni) and thubut (qati) of this proof.
3
Understand the Mu'tazili counterproof
The Mu'tazila argue: accountability is only for a delict (jinaaya), and a delict requires intent (qasd). Since error lacks intent, there is no delict and thus no accountability. What is the logical structure of their argument?
4
Trace the Hanafi response: negligence as delict and shubha
The Hanafi rebuts: error involves a delict of abandoning verification (tark al-tathabbut) and obligatory precaution (ihtiyat). However, due to a semblance of doubt (shubha), hadd and qisas are dropped, but financial liability (daman) remains. Identify the dual reasoning: delict exists, but shubha removes penal consequences.
5
Apply the same principle to divorce
The author extends the ruling: an erroneous divorce (e.g., uttering 'You are divorced' without intent) is valid according to Hanafis, unlike al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi justification: negligence of meaning is subtle, so the fact of mature speech (bulugh) substitutes for intent. Contrast with the case of a sleeping person, where no negligence exists.

مسألة جواز المؤاخذة بالخطأ في الدنيا عقلا المؤاخذة بالخطاء جائزة عقلاً ، خلافاً للمعتزلة لنا قوله تعالى: (ربنا لا تؤاخذنا ان نسينا او اخطأنا والسوال بما يستحيل باطل قالوا: المواخذة بالجناية، وهي بالقصد قلنا بعدم التثبت والاحتياط الواجبين، إلا أن فيه شبهة العدم، فلا يواخذ بحد ولا قصاص دون ضمان المتلفات خطاء من الأموال ويقع طلاقه عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي؛ لأن اعتبار الكلام بالقصد ولم ،يوجد، كما في النائم قلنا: الغفلة عن معنى اللفظ أمر خفى فأقيم تمييز البلوغ مقامه بخلاف النوم المؤاخذة بالخطأ في الدنيا) جائزة عقلاً ( عندنا) خلافاً للمعتزلة، فانهم لا يجوزونها. والدليل لنا : قوله تعالى ربنا لا تؤاخذنا إن نيسنا او أخطأنا والسوال وطلب العفو عما يستحيل باطل ، ولوكانت المؤاخذة على الخط ممنوعة عقلاً لم يأمرنا الله تعالى بسؤال العفو و طلب المغفرة . دليل المعتزلة : قالوا: المؤاخذة إنما تكون بالجناية ، وهي (الجناية تكون بالقصد، ولا قصد في الخطأ فلا جناية ولا مؤاخذة وأجاب عنه المصنف بقوله : قلنا: لا نسلم أنه لا جناية في الخطأ، بل فيه جناية بعدم التثبت أي بترك التثبت وترك الاحتياط الواجبين، إلا أن فيه (في الخطأ)، شبهة عدم الجناية؛ لأنه صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : " إن الله تجاوز عن أمنى الخطأ والنسيان وما أكرهوا عليه فلا يؤاخذ صاحب الخطأ بحد ولا قصاص (لأنهما يسقطان بالشبهات،
Issue: The permissibility of holding to account for error in this world rationally. Holding to account for error is rationally permissible, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Our proof is the statement of Allah: 'Our Lord, do not hold us to account if we forget or err.' And a request for what is rationally impossible is invalid. They (Mu'tazila) said: Accountability is only for a delict, and a delict is by intent. We said: it is not positively proven that there is no delict in error; rather, there is a delict in it by abandoning verification (tathabbut) and the obligatory precaution, except that in it there is a semblance (shubha) of absence, so one is not punished with a hadd penalty or retaliation, but liability for property lost by mistake is not excluded, and his divorce occurs according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i; because speech is considered by intent and it is not present, as in a sleeping person. We said: negligence of the meaning of the utterance is a subtle matter, so the discernment of maturity is established in its place, unlike sleep. Accountability for error in this world is rationally permissible according to us, contrary to the Mu'tazila, for they do not permit it. Our proof: the statement of Allah, 'Our Lord, do not hold us to account if we forget or err,' and a request and seeking of pardon for what is impossible is invalid; if accountability for error were rationally forbidden, Allah would not have commanded us to ask for pardon and forgiveness. The Mu'tazila's proof: They said: accountability is only for a delict, and a delict is by intent; there is no intent in error, so no delict and no accountability. The author responded: We do not concede that there is no delict in error; rather, in it there is a delict by abandoning verification and abandoning the obligatory precaution. Except that in error there is a semblance of absence of delict; because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Allah has overlooked for my ummah error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into.' So the one in error is not held to account with a hadd penalty or retaliation, because they are dropped by doubts.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage addresses a theological-legal dispute between the Hanafi school (following Ahl al-Sunnah) and the Mu'tazila regarding whether it is rationally possible for Allah to hold someone accountable (mu'akhadha) for an act committed by mistake (khata') in this world. The Hanafi position, as presented by the author, is that it is rationally permissible (ja'iz 'aqlan). The proof is the Quranic supplication 'Our Lord, do not hold us to account if we forget or err' – if accountability for error were rationally impossible, asking for its exemption would be invalid. The Mu'tazila argue that accountability only applies to a delict (jinaaya), and a delict requires intent (qasd), which is absent in error. The Hanafi response distinguishes: there is still a delict in error through negligence (tark al-tathabbut wa al-ihtiyat al-wajibayn). However, due to a semblance of doubt (shubha), the hadd penalties and retaliation (qisas) are waived, but financial liability (daman) remains. The author also adds a side dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the validity of divorce by an erroneous utterance: Hanafis rule the divorce occurs due to the subtlety of negligence, while Shafi'is require intent, comparing it to a sleeping person. The passage concludes by reiterating the Hanafi position and the hadith 'Allah has overlooked for my ummah error, forgetfulness, and coercion' as a basis for not applying hadd/qisas in cases of error.

Key Terms
المؤاخذة بالخطأ (al-mu'akhadha bi al-khata'): Holding someone accountable or punishing them for an act done mistakenly.
الجناية (al-jinaya): A delict or transgression that warrants punishment or liability.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance or doubt that prevents the application of hadd penalties or retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the Prophet explicitly commanded punishment for errors.
B) Because the Quranic request for forgiveness implies that accountability is possible.
C) Because error is always intentional.
D) Because the Mu'tazila admit it is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms the rational possibility of accountability for error, using negligence as a basis for liability, but penal sanctions are dropped due to shubha, while civil liability may remain.
